The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying the Three Terms in a Categorical Syllogism
Author introduces the minor, major, and middle terms in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism from Islamic theological or legal texts.

Core Idea
The minor is the subject of the conclusion; the major is the predicate; the middle connects them.

Every categorical syllogism consists of three terms: the minor (asghar), the major (akbar), and the middle (wasat). The author introduces these terms and explains their roles. In this lesson, we will learn how to locate each term in a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is the proposition the syllogism aims to prove. It contains two terms: the subject (minor) and the predicate (major).
In the source, the author says 'wa dhatu haddin asghari: sughrahuma' meaning the term that has a minor limit is their minor.
2
Identify the minor term
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. It is called 'asghar' not because of smaller extension, but as a technical term.
The source clarifies that 'asghar' is not a true comparative but a technical term.
3
Identify the major term
The major term is the predicate of the conclusion, called 'akbar'.
Similarly, 'akbar' is technical.
4
Find the middle term
The term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion is the middle term (wasat). It connects the minor and major.
The source states 'wa wasatun yulgha lad al-intaj' meaning the middle is omitted at production.
5
Practice with a fiqh example
Take 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Conclusion: 'nabidh is forbidden', so minor = nabidh, major = forbidden, middle = intoxicant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ: صُغْرَاهُمَا وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ: كُبْرَاهُمَا. وَهَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ مُتَحَقِّقٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَاءِ أَيْضاً بِتَأْوِيلِهِ بِالافْتِرَاني؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي نَحْوِ «لَوْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ: هَذَا إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» كَذَا قِيلَ. وَبَحَثَ فِيهِ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ: بِأَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ لِذَلِكَ؛ لأَنَّ مَدَارَ الإِنْتَاجِ فِي الاسْتِثْنَانِي لَيْسَ عَلَى هَذَا، بَلْ عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ المَلْزُومِ ثُبُوتُ لَازِمِهِ، أَوْ نَحْوُ ذَلِكَ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. قَوْلُهُ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ) بِالتَّنْوِينِ؛ لِلضَّرُورَةِ، وَكَذَا قَوْلِهِ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ). وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ جَرَى عَلَى أَلْسِنَتِهِمْ: «أَصْغَرُ، وَصُغْرَى، وَأَكْبَرُ، وَكُبْرَى»، وَلَيْسَ بِلَحْنٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يُرِيدُونَ تَفْضِيلاً عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ، وَإِنَّمَا يُرِيدُونَ مَعْنَى فَاعِلٍ وَفَاعِلَةٍ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ النَّحَوِيِّينَ: «جُمْلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَجُمْلَةٌ كُبْرَى»، وَقَوْلِ العَرُوضِيِّينَ: «فَاصِلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَفَاصِلَةٌ كُبْرَى». واسْتُشْكِلَ هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ: بِأَنَّ الأَوْسَطَ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُسَاوِياً لِلأَصْغَرِ؛ نَحْوُ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَسَّاسٌ»، وَأَحَدُ المُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لَا يَصْدُقُ أَنَّهُ مُنْدَرِجٌ فِي صَاحِبِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى انْدِرَاجِ شَيْءٍ فِي شَيْءٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ الثَّانِي شَامِلاً لِلأَوَّلِ وَلِغَيْرِهِ. وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ: بِأَنَّ مَرْجِعَ القِيَاسِ إِلَى اسْتِفَادَةِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى ذَاتِ الأَصْغَرِ بِمَفْهُومِ الأَوْسَطِ، وَهُوَ أَعَمُّ قَطْعاً، وَإِنْ كَانَ مَفْهُومُ الأَصْغَرِ مُسَاوِياً. ثُمَّ قَالَ: (وَأَصْغَرُ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ) أَيْ: فِي الأَكْبَرِ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَوَسَطُ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا أَتَى بِهِ لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِ إِلَى المَطْلُوبِ، فَعِنْدَ الوُصُولِ إِلَيْهِ لَا يُؤْتَى بِهِ، فَهُوَ كَالآلَةِ فِي ذَلِكَ.
And a term that has a minor limit: their minor, and a term that has a major limit: their major. And this inclusion is also realized in the exceptive [syllogism] by interpreting it as conjunctive; such that one says in something like 'If this were a human, it would be an animal, but it is a human: this is a human, and every human is an animal' – thus it was said. And some investigators examined this: that there is no need for that, because the basis of production in the exceptive [syllogism] is not on this, but rather on the fact that from the affirmation of the necessary condition, the affirmation of its necessary consequent follows, or something similar, as will come. His saying: (And a term that has a minor limit) with nunation due to necessity, and similarly his saying: (And a term that has a major limit). And know that it is common on their tongues: 'asghar', 'sughra', 'akbar', 'kubra', and it is not ungrammatical; because they do not intend comparison in the sense of 'min', but rather they intend the meaning of active participle (fa'il) and active participle feminine (fa'ila), as in the grammarians' saying: 'a minor sentence (jumla sughra) and a major sentence (jumla kubra)', and the prosodists' saying: 'a minor foot (fasila sughra) and a major foot (fasila kubra)'. And this inclusion was objected to: that the middle term may be equal to the minor; for example: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is sentient', and one of two equal things cannot be truly said to be included in the other; because the meaning of inclusion of a thing in a thing is that the second thing encompasses the first and others. And al-Sa'd answered: that the basis of the syllogism is to derive the judgment on the subject of the minor through the concept of the middle, and it is broader absolutely, even if the concept of the minor is equal. Then he said: (And the minor: that is possessing inclusion) i.e., in the major. His saying: (And the middle is omitted at the point of production) i.e., because it is only brought to reach the desired conclusion, so when reaching it, it is not brought, thus it is like a tool in that.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an explication of the structure of categorical syllogisms, specifically the roles of the minor term (asghar), major term (akbar), and middle term (wasat). The commentary begins by addressing the linguistic form of 'asghar' and 'akbar', noting that they are not true comparatives (with 'min') but rather act as participles (fa'il and fa'ila), similar to grammatical terms like 'jumla sughra' (minor sentence) and 'fasila sughra' (minor prosodic foot). The author then discusses 'inclusion' (indiraj) in the context of syllogism. A conditional syllogism (istithna'i) can be reinterpreted as a categorical one (iqtirani) by converting its parts into a categorical form. However, some investigators argue that this conversion is unnecessary because the mechanism of inference in conditional syllogisms is based on the principle of entailment (luzum) rather than inclusion of terms. An objection is raised: the middle term may be coextensive (musawi) with the minor term, as in 'Every human is rational, every rational is sentient.' In such a case, the concept of the middle is not broader, so inclusion (where the middle encompasses the minor and others) seems false. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) responds that the syllogism's purpose is to derive a judgment about the individual of the minor term through the concept of the middle. Even if the concepts are equal in extension, the middle term is still conceptually broader in the sense that it is the means of connection. The passage then quotes the matn line: 'And the minor: that is possessing inclusion' meaning the minor term is included in the major in affirmative syllogisms. Finally, it states that the middle term is omitted (mulgha) at the point of conclusion because it serves only as a tool to connect the minor and major; once the conclusion is reached, it is no longer mentioned. The commentary here uses an example from kalam: 'Every human is an animal' etc. but avoids secular examples.

Key Terms
أَصْغَر
أَكْبَر
وَسَط
انْدِرَاج
إِنْتَاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram', what is the major term?
A) intoxicant
B) haram
C) nabidh
D) syllogism

Key Takeaway
The three terms of a categorical syllogism are defined by their roles in the conclusion and premises: minor (subject of conclusion), major (predicate of conclusion), middle (present in both premises, omitted in conclusion).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following syllogism from kalam: 'Every temporally originated thing is contingent; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world is contingent.' Identify the minor, major, and middle terms.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Inclusion of the Minor in the Major: The Principle and the Coextension Objection
Author discusses the inclusion of minor in major and resolves the coextension objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of inclusion (indiraj) in categorical syllogisms and how al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) resolves the objection when the minor and middle terms are coextensive.

Core Idea
Inclusion is conceptual, not extensional; the middle is always broader in concept.

The author states that the minor term is included in the major term. But an objection arises: what if the middle term is coextensive with the minor? How can the minor be 'included' if they are equal? This lesson presents the objection and al-Sa'd's elegant solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'inclusion' (indiraj) in syllogism
Inclusion means that the major term encompasses the minor term and possibly others. In an affirmative syllogism, the minor is included under the major via the middle term.
The source says 'wa dhatu haddin asghari... wa hadha al-indiraj'.
2
Identify the objection
If the minor and middle are coextensive, like 'human' and 'rational', then the middle is not broader, so inclusion seems false.
The source states 'ushtakila hadha al-indiraj bi anna al-awsata qad yakunu musawiyan lil asghar'.
3
Find al-Sa'd's answer
Al-Sa'd argues that the syllogism derives a judgment on the subject of the minor through the concept (mafhum) of the middle, and the concept is always broader, even if extensionally equal.
The source says 'fa inna marji'a al-qiyas ila istifadati al-hukmi 'ala dhati al-asghari bi mafhumi al-awsati wa huwa a'ammu qat'an'.
4
Apply to an example
Consider 'Every human is rational; every rational is capable of abstraction.' 'Human' and 'rational' are coextensive, yet the middle 'rational' is conceptually broader as it could apply to non-human rational beings. The syllogism is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ: صُغْرَاهُمَا وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ: كُبْرَاهُمَا. وَهَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ مُتَحَقِّقٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَاءِ أَيْضاً بِتَأْوِيلِهِ بِالافْتِرَاني؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي نَحْوِ «لَوْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ: هَذَا إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» كَذَا قِيلَ. وَبَحَثَ فِيهِ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ: بِأَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ لِذَلِكَ؛ لأَنَّ مَدَارَ الإِنْتَاجِ فِي الاسْتِثْنَانِي لَيْسَ عَلَى هَذَا، بَلْ عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ المَلْزُومِ ثُبُوتُ لَازِمِهِ، أَوْ نَحْوُ ذَلِكَ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. قَوْلُهُ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ) بِالتَّنْوِينِ؛ لِلضَّرُورَةِ، وَكَذَا قَوْلِهِ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ). وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ جَرَى عَلَى أَلْسِنَتِهِمْ: «أَصْغَرُ، وَصُغْرَى، وَأَكْبَرُ، وَكُبْرَى»، وَلَيْسَ بِلَحْنٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يُرِيدُونَ تَفْضِيلاً عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ، وَإِنَّمَا يُرِيدُونَ مَعْنَى فَاعِلٍ وَفَاعِلَةٍ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ النَّحَوِيِّينَ: «جُمْلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَجُمْلَةٌ كُبْرَى»، وَقَوْلِ العَرُوضِيِّينَ: «فَاصِلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَفَاصِلَةٌ كُبْرَى». واسْتُشْكِلَ هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ: بِأَنَّ الأَوْسَطَ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُسَاوِياً لِلأَصْغَرِ؛ نَحْوُ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَسَّاسٌ»، وَأَحَدُ المُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لَا يَصْدُقُ أَنَّهُ مُنْدَرِجٌ فِي صَاحِبِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى انْدِرَاجِ شَيْءٍ فِي شَيْءٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ الثَّانِي شَامِلاً لِلأَوَّلِ وَلِغَيْرِهِ. وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ: بِأَنَّ مَرْجِعَ القِيَاسِ إِلَى اسْتِفَادَةِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى ذَاتِ الأَصْغَرِ بِمَفْهُومِ الأَوْسَطِ، وَهُوَ أَعَمُّ قَطْعاً، وَإِنْ كَانَ مَفْهُومُ الأَصْغَرِ مُسَاوِياً. ثُمَّ قَالَ: (وَأَصْغَرُ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ) أَيْ: فِي الأَكْبَرِ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَوَسَطُ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا أَتَى بِهِ لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِ إِلَى المَطْلُوبِ، فَعِنْدَ الوُصُولِ إِلَيْهِ لَا يُؤْتَى بِهِ، فَهُوَ كَالآلَةِ فِي ذَلِكَ.
And a term that has a minor limit: their minor, and a term that has a major limit: their major. And this inclusion is also realized in the exceptive [syllogism] by interpreting it as conjunctive; such that one says in something like 'If this were a human, it would be an animal, but it is a human: this is a human, and every human is an animal' – thus it was said. And some investigators examined this: that there is no need for that, because the basis of production in the exceptive [syllogism] is not on this, but rather on the fact that from the affirmation of the necessary condition, the affirmation of its necessary consequent follows, or something similar, as will come. His saying: (And a term that has a minor limit) with nunation due to necessity, and similarly his saying: (And a term that has a major limit). And know that it is common on their tongues: 'asghar', 'sughra', 'akbar', 'kubra', and it is not ungrammatical; because they do not intend comparison in the sense of 'min', but rather they intend the meaning of active participle (fa'il) and active participle feminine (fa'ila), as in the grammarians' saying: 'a minor sentence (jumla sughra) and a major sentence (jumla kubra)', and the prosodists' saying: 'a minor foot (fasila sughra) and a major foot (fasila kubra)'. And this inclusion was objected to: that the middle term may be equal to the minor; for example: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is sentient', and one of two equal things cannot be truly said to be included in the other; because the meaning of inclusion of a thing in a thing is that the second thing encompasses the first and others. And al-Sa'd answered: that the basis of the syllogism is to derive the judgment on the subject of the minor through the concept of the middle, and it is broader absolutely, even if the concept of the minor is equal. Then he said: (And the minor: that is possessing inclusion) i.e., in the major. His saying: (And the middle is omitted at the point of production) i.e., because it is only brought to reach the desired conclusion, so when reaching it, it is not brought, thus it is like a tool in that.

Commentary
This passage is an explication of the structure of categorical syllogisms, specifically the roles of the minor term (asghar), major term (akbar), and middle term (wasat). The commentary begins by addressing the linguistic form of 'asghar' and 'akbar', noting that they are not true comparatives (with 'min') but rather act as participles (fa'il and fa'ila), similar to grammatical terms like 'jumla sughra' (minor sentence) and 'fasila sughra' (minor prosodic foot). The author then discusses 'inclusion' (indiraj) in the context of syllogism. A conditional syllogism (istithna'i) can be reinterpreted as a categorical one (iqtirani) by converting its parts into a categorical form. However, some investigators argue that this conversion is unnecessary because the mechanism of inference in conditional syllogisms is based on the principle of entailment (luzum) rather than inclusion of terms. An objection is raised: the middle term may be coextensive (musawi) with the minor term, as in 'Every human is rational, every rational is sentient.' In such a case, the concept of the middle is not broader, so inclusion (where the middle encompasses the minor and others) seems false. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) responds that the syllogism's purpose is to derive a judgment about the individual of the minor term through the concept of the middle. Even if the concepts are equal in extension, the middle term is still conceptually broader in the sense that it is the means of connection. The passage then quotes the matn line: 'And the minor: that is possessing inclusion' meaning the minor term is included in the major in affirmative syllogisms. Finally, it states that the middle term is omitted (mulgha) at the point of conclusion because it serves only as a tool to connect the minor and major; once the conclusion is reached, it is no longer mentioned. The commentary here uses an example from kalam: 'Every human is an animal' etc. but avoids secular examples.

Checkpoint
What does al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) mean when he says the middle term is 'conceptually broader' (a'ammu bi al-mafhum)?
A) The middle term refers to a larger set of objects in the world
B) The middle term's definition applies to a wider range of possible beings than the minor
C) The middle term is always grammatically longer
D) The middle term is used more often in speech

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the minor in the major is not about extensional breadth but about the conceptual generality of the middle term, preserving syllogistic validity even when terms are coextensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every created thing has a beginning; the world is created; therefore the world has a beginning.' The minor 'world' and middle 'created thing' are often considered coextensive in Islamic theology (everything other than God is created). Explain how al-Sa'd's solution applies here.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Omission of the Middle Term in the Conclusion
Author states that the middle term is omitted at the point of production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the middle term does not appear in the conclusion of a valid categorical syllogism and identify the middle term's role as a tool.

Core Idea
The middle term is a bridge that disappears once the conclusion is reached.

Why does the conclusion of a syllogism only contain the minor and major terms? The author explains that the middle term is omitted because its only purpose is to connect the premises. Once that connection yields the conclusion, the middle term is no longer needed.

Guided Path
1
Recall structure of a categorical syllogism
A categorical syllogism has two premises and a conclusion. The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
The source states 'wa wasatun yulgha lad al-intaj'.
2
Identify the function of the middle term
The middle term serves as a mediator (wasila) to link the minor and major terms. Without it, no inference is possible.
The author compares it to a tool (ka al-alah).
3
Understand why it is omitted
Once the conclusion is derived, the middle term has fulfilled its role. Including it in the conclusion would be redundant and would not produce a standard syllogistic form.
The source says 'innama ati bihi li yatawassala bihi ila al-matlub, fa 'inda al-wusul ilayhi la yu'ta bihi'.
4
Verify with a fiqh example
In 'Every intoxicant is haram; nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is haram', the middle term 'intoxicant' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.

Source Text

وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ: صُغْرَاهُمَا وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ: كُبْرَاهُمَا. وَهَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ مُتَحَقِّقٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَاءِ أَيْضاً بِتَأْوِيلِهِ بِالافْتِرَاني؛ بِأَنْ يُقَالَ فِي نَحْوِ «لَوْ كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ: هَذَا إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ» كَذَا قِيلَ. وَبَحَثَ فِيهِ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ: بِأَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ لِذَلِكَ؛ لأَنَّ مَدَارَ الإِنْتَاجِ فِي الاسْتِثْنَانِي لَيْسَ عَلَى هَذَا، بَلْ عَلَى أَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ المَلْزُومِ ثُبُوتُ لَازِمِهِ، أَوْ نَحْوُ ذَلِكَ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. قَوْلُهُ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَصْغَرِ) بِالتَّنْوِينِ؛ لِلضَّرُورَةِ، وَكَذَا قَوْلِهِ: (وَذَاتُ حَدٌ أَكْبَرِ). وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ جَرَى عَلَى أَلْسِنَتِهِمْ: «أَصْغَرُ، وَصُغْرَى، وَأَكْبَرُ، وَكُبْرَى»، وَلَيْسَ بِلَحْنٍ؛ لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يُرِيدُونَ تَفْضِيلاً عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ، وَإِنَّمَا يُرِيدُونَ مَعْنَى فَاعِلٍ وَفَاعِلَةٍ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ النَّحَوِيِّينَ: «جُمْلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَجُمْلَةٌ كُبْرَى»، وَقَوْلِ العَرُوضِيِّينَ: «فَاصِلَةٌ صُغْرَى، وَفَاصِلَةٌ كُبْرَى». واسْتُشْكِلَ هَذَا الاِنْدِرَاجُ: بِأَنَّ الأَوْسَطَ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُسَاوِياً لِلأَصْغَرِ؛ نَحْوُ: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَسَّاسٌ»، وَأَحَدُ المُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لَا يَصْدُقُ أَنَّهُ مُنْدَرِجٌ فِي صَاحِبِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ مَعْنَى انْدِرَاجِ شَيْءٍ فِي شَيْءٍ أَنْ يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ الثَّانِي شَامِلاً لِلأَوَّلِ وَلِغَيْرِهِ. وَأَجَابَ السَّعْدُ: بِأَنَّ مَرْجِعَ القِيَاسِ إِلَى اسْتِفَادَةِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى ذَاتِ الأَصْغَرِ بِمَفْهُومِ الأَوْسَطِ، وَهُوَ أَعَمُّ قَطْعاً، وَإِنْ كَانَ مَفْهُومُ الأَصْغَرِ مُسَاوِياً. ثُمَّ قَالَ: (وَأَصْغَرُ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ) أَيْ: فِي الأَكْبَرِ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَوَسَطُ يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا أَتَى بِهِ لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِ إِلَى المَطْلُوبِ، فَعِنْدَ الوُصُولِ إِلَيْهِ لَا يُؤْتَى بِهِ، فَهُوَ كَالآلَةِ فِي ذَلِكَ.
And a term that has a minor limit: their minor, and a term that has a major limit: their major. And this inclusion is also realized in the exceptive [syllogism] by interpreting it as conjunctive; such that one says in something like 'If this were a human, it would be an animal, but it is a human: this is a human, and every human is an animal' – thus it was said. And some investigators examined this: that there is no need for that, because the basis of production in the exceptive [syllogism] is not on this, but rather on the fact that from the affirmation of the necessary condition, the affirmation of its necessary consequent follows, or something similar, as will come. His saying: (And a term that has a minor limit) with nunation due to necessity, and similarly his saying: (And a term that has a major limit). And know that it is common on their tongues: 'asghar', 'sughra', 'akbar', 'kubra', and it is not ungrammatical; because they do not intend comparison in the sense of 'min', but rather they intend the meaning of active participle (fa'il) and active participle feminine (fa'ila), as in the grammarians' saying: 'a minor sentence (jumla sughra) and a major sentence (jumla kubra)', and the prosodists' saying: 'a minor foot (fasila sughra) and a major foot (fasila kubra)'. And this inclusion was objected to: that the middle term may be equal to the minor; for example: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is sentient', and one of two equal things cannot be truly said to be included in the other; because the meaning of inclusion of a thing in a thing is that the second thing encompasses the first and others. And al-Sa'd answered: that the basis of the syllogism is to derive the judgment on the subject of the minor through the concept of the middle, and it is broader absolutely, even if the concept of the minor is equal. Then he said: (And the minor: that is possessing inclusion) i.e., in the major. His saying: (And the middle is omitted at the point of production) i.e., because it is only brought to reach the desired conclusion, so when reaching it, it is not brought, thus it is like a tool in that.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is an explication of the structure of categorical syllogisms, specifically the roles of the minor term (asghar), major term (akbar), and middle term (wasat). The commentary begins by addressing the linguistic form of 'asghar' and 'akbar', noting that they are not true comparatives (with 'min') but rather act as participles (fa'il and fa'ila), similar to grammatical terms like 'jumla sughra' (minor sentence) and 'fasila sughra' (minor prosodic foot). The author then discusses 'inclusion' (indiraj) in the context of syllogism. A conditional syllogism (istithna'i) can be reinterpreted as a categorical one (iqtirani) by converting its parts into a categorical form. However, some investigators argue that this conversion is unnecessary because the mechanism of inference in conditional syllogisms is based on the principle of entailment (luzum) rather than inclusion of terms. An objection is raised: the middle term may be coextensive (musawi) with the minor term, as in 'Every human is rational, every rational is sentient.' In such a case, the concept of the middle is not broader, so inclusion (where the middle encompasses the minor and others) seems false. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) responds that the syllogism's purpose is to derive a judgment about the individual of the minor term through the concept of the middle. Even if the concepts are equal in extension, the middle term is still conceptually broader in the sense that it is the means of connection. The passage then quotes the matn line: 'And the minor: that is possessing inclusion' meaning the minor term is included in the major in affirmative syllogisms. Finally, it states that the middle term is omitted (mulgha) at the point of conclusion because it serves only as a tool to connect the minor and major; once the conclusion is reached, it is no longer mentioned. The commentary here uses an example from kalam: 'Every human is an animal' etc. but avoids secular examples.

Key Terms
وَسَط
إِنْتَاج
انْدِرَاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid categorical syllogism, where does the middle term appear?
A) Only in the minor premise
B) In both premises but not in the conclusion
C) In the conclusion and one premise
D) Nowhere, it is optional

Key Takeaway
The middle term is the essential link in a categorical syllogism, but it does not appear in the conclusion because its function is only to connect the minor and major terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following qiyas: 'Silk is not impure; milk is not impure; therefore silk is permissible to wear in prayer (based on a principle).' Is this a valid categorical syllogism? Identify the middle term and note its omission in the conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Minor and Middle Terms in the Syllogism
Commentary explains the meaning of 'asghar' and 'wasat' and addresses an objection about negative syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor and middle terms in a categorical syllogism and interpret 'inclusion' (indiraj) in both affirmative and negative syllogisms.

Core Idea
In a negative syllogism, 'inclusion' means the negation of the major applies to the minor.

This passage from a commentary on a matn explains the roles of the minor term (asghar) and the middle term (wasat) in a syllogism. The commentator clarifies what 'inclusion' means and addresses a potential objection about negative syllogisms, showing that the concept extends beyond mere class membership to cover the extension of negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms in a syllogism
Given a categorical syllogism, locate the subject and predicate of the conclusion (minor and major) and the term that appears in both premises (middle). For example, in 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone', the minor is 'human', major is 'stone', middle is 'animal'.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion; the major is the predicate.
2
Understand the literal meaning of 'inclusion'
In affirmative syllogisms, the minor term is a subset of the major term. The commentator calls this 'indiraj', meaning the minor falls under the extension of the major.
This is straightforward for positive statements.
3
Apply inclusion to negative syllogisms
When the conclusion is negative, the minor is not a subset of the major. The commentator explains that 'inclusion' here means the negation of the major extends over the minor, i.e., the minor is excluded from the major's extension.
The objection raised in the passage is solved by this reinterpretation.
4
Explain why the middle term is dropped
The middle term serves as a tool to connect the premises; once the conclusion is reached, it is no longer needed and is omitted from the conclusion.
The middle term is like a ladder that is removed after climbing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القياس
وَأَصْغَرُ فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ وَوَسَطٌ يُلْغَى لَدَى الباجوري في الإِنْتَاجِ
قَوْلُهُ : (وَ«أَصْغَرٌ» فَذَاكَ ذُو انْدِرَاجِ أَيْ: فِي الأَكْبَرِ ، كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ المُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِهِ وَبِحَمْلِ الاِنْدِرَاجِ هُنَا عَلَى انْدِرَاجِ الأَصْغَرِ فِي الأَكْبَرِ، مَعَ حَمْلِهِ فِيمَا سَبَقَ عَلَى انْدِرَاجِهِ الأَوْسَطِ ، انْدَفَعَ - كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ - الاعْتِرَاضُ عَلَى المُصَنْفِ بِأَنَّ فِي كَلَامِهِ تِكْرَاراً. فَإِنْ قِيلَ : انْدِرَاجُ الأَصْغَرِ فِي الأَكْبَرِ لَا يَتَأَنَّى فِي السَّلْبِ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ» ؛ لِأَنَّ الحَدَّ الأَكْبَرَ مُبَايِنُ لِلأَصْغَرِ . أُجِيبَ بِمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ: مِنْ أَنَّ مَعْنَى انْدِرَاجِهِ فِيهِ فِي صُورَةِ السَّلْبِ: انْسِحَابُ سَلْبِ الأَكْبَرِ عَلَيْهِ، فَتَأمَّل.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَوَسَطُ» يُلْغَى لَدَى الإِنْتَاجِ أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا أَتَى بِهِ لِيَتَوَصَّلَ بِهِ إِلَى المَطْلُوبِ، فَعِنْدَ الوُصُولِ إِلَيْهِ لَا يُؤْتَى بِهِ، فَهُوَ كَالَآلَةِ فِي ذَلِكَ.
Chapter on the Syllogism. 'And the minor term is that which is included, and the middle term is dropped at the conclusion.' His saying: ('And the minor term is that which is included') means: in the major term, as the author clarified in his commentary. And by interpreting 'inclusion' here as the inclusion of the minor in the major, while interpreting it previously as the inclusion of the middle term, the objection against the author that his statement contains repetition is repelled, as some investigators have said. For if it is said: The inclusion of the minor in the major does not occur in negative [syllogisms] like 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone' because the major term is separate from the minor, it is answered by what some have said: The meaning of its inclusion in it in the case of a negative is the extension of the negation of the major over it. So reflect. His saying: ('And the middle term is dropped at the conclusion') means: because it is only brought to serve as a means to reach the desired conclusion, so when the conclusion is reached it is not mentioned; thus it is like a tool in that.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line explaining the roles of the minor term (asghar) and the middle term (wasat) in a syllogism. The commentator first clarifies that 'asghar' refers to the term that is included in the major term. He notes that the author's use of 'inclusion' here is specific to the minor's inclusion in the major, whereas earlier it referred to the middle term's inclusion, thus avoiding an objection of repetition. An objection is then raised: in negative syllogisms (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone'), the minor 'human' is not actually included in the major 'stone' because they are separate. The response is that 'inclusion' in such cases means the negation of the major applies to the minor—that is, the property of being excluded from the major extends to the minor. The commentator then turns to the middle term, explaining that it is omitted from the conclusion because it serves only as a tool to connect the premises and derive the conclusion; once the conclusion is reached, the middle term is no longer needed.

Key Terms
أَصْغَر
أَكْبَر
وَسَط
انْدِرَاج

Checkpoint
In a negative syllogism such as 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone,' what does 'indiraj' (inclusion) of the minor 'human' in the major 'stone' mean?
A) The minor is a subclass of the major.
B) The negation of the major ('stone') applies to the minor ('human').
C) The minor and major are identical in meaning.
D) The middle term is included in the major.

Key Takeaway
In affirmative syllogisms, the minor is included under the major; in negative syllogisms, the negation of the major extends over the minor.

?

The Syllogism

Defining Figure and Distinguishing from Mood
Commentary defines 'shakl' and distinguishes it from 'darb'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism by its term arrangement and distinguish it from the mood based on quantifiers.

Core Idea
Figure is about term order; mood is about quantifiers and quality.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians define the 'figure' (shakl) of a syllogism and differentiate it from the 'mood' (darb). The commentary unpacks the matn's definition, emphasizing that figure concerns only the arrangement of the minor, middle, and major terms, while mood takes into account the quantifiers (aswar) and the quality of the premises. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly classifying and evaluating syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum in the Arabic
Find the term being defined (al-shakl) in the Arabic passage. Notice that the definition begins with 'al-shaklu 'inda ha'ula'i al-nasi yutlaqu 'an qadiyyatay qiyasin'.
The term appears at the start of the matn quotation.
2
Extract the defining characteristics
The definition states that figure is applied to two premises of a syllogism (qadiyyatay qiyas) without considering the quantifiers (min ghayri an tu'tabara al-aswar). This means figure depends solely on how the terms (subject, predicate, middle) are arranged.
The commentary emphasizes that the two premises must form a syllogism, not just any two propositions.
3
Contrast figure with mood
The commentary explicitly contrasts figure with mood: while figure ignores quantifiers, mood (darb) requires consideration of quantifiers. The commenter states that between figure and mood there is 'complete difference' (al-tabayun).
Mood is defined by the combination of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises.
4
Test the definition with a non-syllogistic example
The commentary gives a counterexample: 'Every human is an animal, and every horse is a neighing one.' These two premises share no common term, so they are not a syllogism, and their form is not called a figure. This clarifies that only syllogistic pairs qualify.
This test helps solidify the condition that the premises must share a middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الأَشْكَالِ
الشَّكْلُ عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ يُطْلَقُ عَنْ قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ لَهُ يُشَارُ
قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ فِي الأَشْكَالِ أَيْ : فِي بَيَانِهَا ، وَبَيَانِ شُرُوطِهَا، وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ : ( عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ يَعْنِي : المَنَاطِقَةِ، وَقَيَّدَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأنَّ الشَّكْلَ عِنْدَ اللُّغَوِيِّينَ لَا يَخْتَصُّ بِذَلِكَ، بَلْ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى هَيئَةِ الشَّيْءِ مُطْلَقاً
قَوْلُهُ : (يُطْلَقُ عَنْ قَضِيَّنَيْ قِيَاسِ أَيْ: عَلَى هَيْئَتِهِمَا الحَاصِلَةِ مِنِ اجْتِمَاعِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الكُبْرَى بِاعْتِبَارِ طَرَفَيِ المَطْلُوبِ مَعَ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ، فَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مَجَازٌ لُغَوِيٌّ وَمَجَازُ بِالحَذْفِ وَاحْتَرَزَ بِقَوْلِهِ : قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسِ عَنْ فَضِيَّنَيْ غَيْرِ قِيَاسِ ؛ كَمَا لَوْ قُلْتَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ صَهَالٍ ، فَلَا تُسَمَّى هَيَّتُهُمَا شَكلاً .
قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ) ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ عَدَمَ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ شَرْطُ فِي الشَّكْلِ، كَمَا أَنَّ اعْتِبَارَهَا شَرْطُ فِي الضَّرْبِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا : فَبَيْنَ الشَّكْلِ وَالضَّرْبِ : التَّبَيُنُ.
الأنبابي
قوله : (وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ) أي : من بيان الضُّروب المنتجة،
A Section on the Figures. 'The figure according to these people is applied to two premises of a syllogism without considering the quantifiers, since by the mood it is pointed to.' His saying: ('A Section on the Figures') means: in explaining them, their conditions, and what pertains to that. His saying: ('According to these people') means: the logicians. He restricted it because 'figure' according to philologists is not specific to that, but is applied to the form of a thing absolutely. His saying: ('Is applied to two premises of a syllogism') means: to their form resulting from the combination of the minor premise with the major premise by considering the two extremes of the desired conclusion along with the middle term. So in the author's statement there is a linguistic metaphor and a metaphor by ellipsis. And he excluded by his saying 'two premises of a syllogism' from two premises that are not a syllogism, as if you say: 'Every human is an animal, and every horse is a neighing one,' then their form is not called a figure. His saying: ('Without considering the quantifiers') its apparent meaning is that non-consideration of the quantifiers is a condition for the figure, just as their consideration is a condition for the mood. On this view, between figure and mood there is complete difference [in concept]. Al-Anbabi: His saying: ('And what pertains to that') means: from explanation of the productive moods.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on the figures (ashkal) of the syllogism. The commentator begins by quoting the matn definition: 'The figure according to these people is applied to two premises of a syllogism without considering the quantifiers, since by the mood it is pointed to.' He then unpacks each part. First, 'according to these people' restricts the term to the logicians, since in ordinary language 'figure' refers to any form. Second, the definition applies only to two premises that form a syllogism, not any two arbitrary propositions (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse is a neighing one' do not form a syllogism because they share no common term). The phrase 'without considering the quantifiers' means that the figure is determined solely by the arrangement of the terms (minor, middle, major) across the two premises, ignoring whether the propositions are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. The quantifiers and quality are what define the mood (darb). Thus, figure and mood are distinct concepts: figure relates to term order, mood to the types of propositions. The commentator also notes a linguistic metaphor in the matn: the word 'عن' is used in the sense of 'على' and there is an ellipsis (the word 'هيئة' is omitted). Finally, al-Anbabi adds that the phrase 'what pertains to that' refers to the explanation of the productive moods within each figure.

Key Terms
الشَّكْل
الضَّرْب
الأَسْوَار

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Figure considers the arrangement of terms; mood considers the quantifiers and quality of the premises.
C) Figure applies only to affirmative syllogisms; mood applies only to negative.

Figure and mood are distinct: figure concerns the arrangement of terms in the premises, while mood concerns the quantifiers and quality of those premises.

Definition of Shakl and its Distinction from Darb
Bajuri defines shakl and distinguishes it from darb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shakl (figure) and darb (mood) in a categorical syllogism by identifying whether quantifiers are considered.

Core Idea
Figure ignores quantifiers; mood considers them — they are mutually exclusive.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Bajuri's commentary that defines the term 'shakl' (figure) as it is used in logic. You will learn the precise technical meaning of 'figure', the conditions that define it, and how it differs from 'darb' (mood). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing the structure of categorical syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'شكل' (shakl) in the Arabic passage. Notice that the author restricts its meaning to the logicians' usage, distinguishing it from the lexicographers' general sense of 'form'.
The restriction is indicated by 'عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ' (according to these people).
2
Extract the definition's components
The definition states that shakl is applied to the two premises of a syllogism, specifically their form resulting from the arrangement of the minor and major with respect to the middle term. Identify the genus (form of two premises of a syllogism) and the differentia (without considering quantifiers).
The phrase 'مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ' is crucial: quantifiers are not considered.
3
Understand the exclusion of non-syllogisms
Note that the definition excludes pairs of propositions that do not form a valid syllogism. The example given is 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse neighs' — these share no middle term and do not form a figure.
The term 'قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسِ' explicitly restricts to premises of a syllogism.
4
Contrast shakl with darb
The passage states that not considering quantifiers is a condition for shakl, while considering them is a condition for darb. This makes them mutually exclusive (tabayun). To distinguish, ask: does the classification involve quantifier type (universal/particular, affirmative/negative)? If yes, it is about mood; if no, it is about figure.
For example, 'Barbara' is a mood (AAA in first figure), while the first figure itself is a shakl irrespective of quantifiers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الأَشْكَالِ
[ قَوْلُهُ : ] (فَصْلٌ فِي الأَشْكَالِ أَيْ : فِي بَيَانِهَا ، وَبَيَانِ شُرُوطِهَا، وَمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ : ( عِنْدَ هَؤُلَاءِ النَّاسِ يَعْنِي : المَنَاطِقَةَ، وَقَيَّدَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأنَّ الشَّكْلَ عِنْدَ اللُّغَوِيِّينَ لَا يَخْتَصُّ بِذَلِكَ، بَلْ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى هَيئَةِ الشَّيْءِ مُطْلَقاً. قَوْلُهُ : (يُطْلَقُ عَنْ قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسِ أَيْ: عَلَى هَيْئَتِهِمَا الحَاصِلَةِ مِنِ اجْتِمَاعِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الكُبْرَى بِاعْتِبَارِ طَرَفَيِ المَطْلُوبِ مَعَ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ، فَفِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مَجَازٌ لُغَوِيٌّ وَمَجَازُ بِالحَذْفِ. وَاحْتَرَزَ بِقَوْلِهِ : قَضِيَّتَيْ قِيَاسِ عَنْ قَضِيَّتَيْ غَيْرِ قِيَاسِ ؛ كَمَا لَوْ قُلْتَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ صَهَالٍ ، فَلَا تُسَمَّى هَيَّتُهُمَا شَكلاً . قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ) ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ عَدَمَ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ شَرْطُ فِي الشَّكْلِ، كَمَا أَنَّ اعْتِبَارَهَا شَرْطُ فِي الضَّرْبِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا : فَبَيْنَ الشَّكْلِ وَالضَّرْبِ : التَّبايُنُ.
A section on the figures. [His saying:] ('A section on the figures') i.e., about their explanation, and explanation of their conditions, and what relates to that. His saying: ('According to these people') means the logicians, and he restricted it because the figure according to lexicographers is not specific to that, but is applied to the form of a thing absolutely. His saying: ('It is applied to the two premises of a syllogism') i.e., to their form resulting from the combination of the minor with the major in consideration of the two ends of the sought (conclusion) with the middle term. So in the author's speech there is a lexical metaphor and a metaphor by ellipsis. And he guarded by his saying ('the two premises of a syllogism') against the two premises of a non-syllogism, as if you said: 'Every human is an animal, and every horse is a whinnying thing,' then their form is not called a figure. His saying: ('Without considering the quantifiers') its apparent meaning is that not considering the quantifiers is a condition for the figure, just as considering them is a condition for the mood. And upon this, between the figure and the mood there is mutual exclusivity.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes a 'shakl' (figure) in logic and distinguishes it from 'darb' (mood). The commentary opens by explaining the section heading: the author means to explain the figures, their conditions, and related matters. The reason for the restriction to logicians is that lexicographers use 'shakl' for any form, but in logic it is a technical term. The figure is defined as the form of the two premises of a syllogism, specifically the arrangement resulting from the combination of the minor and major premises with respect to the two terms of the conclusion (subject and predicate) and the middle term. The author's expression involves a metaphor: 'عن' is used in the sense of 'على'. The term 'qadiyatay qiyas' (the two premises of a syllogism) is used to exclude non-syllogistic pairs; for example, 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every horse neighs' do not form a syllogism, so their arrangement is not a figure. A crucial point is that the quantifiers (aswar) are not considered when determining the figure; they are only considered when specifying the mood. Therefore, the figure is determined solely by the position of the middle term relative to the extremes, ignoring whether the premises are universal, particular, affirmative, or negative. This leads to a relation of complete mutual exclusivity (tabayun) between figure and mood: they are different concepts and no instance of one is an instance of the other.

Key Terms
شكل
ضرب
أسوار
قضيتا قياس

A) Shakl considers quantifiers, darb does not.
B) Shakl does not consider quantifiers, darb does.
C) Shakl is the major premise, darb is the minor.
D) Shakl and darb are identical.

Key Takeaway
The shakl of a syllogism is determined solely by the arrangement of the middle term, ignoring quantifiers; the darb additionally considers quantifiers, making them fully distinct concepts.

The Syllogism

Interpreting 'Without Considering Quantifiers' in Syllogism
Bajuri presents a second interpretation that considers quantifiers optional, leading to umum wa khusus, and discusses the plural of aswar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the relationship between figure and mood under the second interpretation and resolve the objection regarding the plural of quantifiers.

Core Idea
Figure and mood have absolute generality and specificity: every mood is a figure, but not vice versa.

In classical logic, the terms 'figure' and 'mood' are often confused. This passage clarifies their relationship by presenting a key interpretation of the condition 'min ghayri an tu'tabara al-aswar'. Understanding this interpretation is crucial for correctly analyzing syllogisms in Islamic logic. We will explore how this interpretation leads to the relation of umum wa khusus mutlaq and how the plural of quantifiers is justified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of the condition
The first interpretation takes 'min ghayri' literally as 'without considering', meaning quantifiers must be absent. The second interpretation (favored by Sayyid Sa'id) takes it as 'without requiring consideration', allowing quantifiers to be either present or absent.
This shift is subtle but crucial for the relationship between figure and mood.
2
Derive the relationship between figure and mood
Under the second interpretation, a figure is defined solely by the order of terms without considering quantifiers, while a mood includes quantifiers. Since quantifiers are optional for a figure, every mood (which has a figure) is also a figure, but a figure can have multiple moods. Thus, every darb is a shakl, but not every shakl is a darb — absolute generality and specificity.
This is supported by the common phrase 'dharub al-ashkal' (moods of the figures), implying moods are types of figures.
3
Resolve the objection about the plural 'aswar'
An objection: a syllogism has only two quantifiers, so why use the plural 'aswar'? Two answers: (a) the quantifier in itself has four divisions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), so the plural is justified by these types; (b) the plural may be interpreted as a dual, as is occasionally done.
Both answers preserve the correctness of the author's phrasing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَكِنَّ الَّذِي أَفَادَهُ سَيْدِي سَعِيد أَنَّ مَعْنَى قَوْلِ المُصَنِّفِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ : مِنْ غَيْرِ اشْتِرَاطِ اعْتِبَارِهَا ، فَالمَنْفِيُّ إِنَّمَا هُوَ شَرْطُ اعْتِبَارِهَا ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَصْدُقُ بِاعْتِبَارِهَا وَعَدَمِ اعْتِبَارِهَا ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَبَيْنَ الشَّكْلِ وَالضَّرْبِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ  لِأَنَّ كُلَّ ضَرْبٍ شَكُلِّ، وَلَا عَكْسُ، وَاسْتَبْعَدَ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ ذَلِكَ مِنْ كَلامِ المُصَنِّفِ ، لَكِنَّهُ هُوَ الأَنْسَبُ بِقَوْلِهِمْ: ضُرُوبُ الأَشْكَالِ»؛ أَيْ : أَنْوَاعُهَا . فَإِنْ قِيلَ : القِيَاسُ لَا يَشْتَمِلُ إِلَّا عَلَى سُورَيْنِ ، فَلِمَ جَمَعَ المُصَنِّفُ حَيْثُ قَالَ: مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ»؟ أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ جَمَعَ؛ نَظَراً إِلَى أَنَّ السُّورَ فِي حَدِّ ذَاتِهِ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَام : السُّورُ الكُلِّيُّ الإِيجَابِيُّ وَالسَّلْبِيُّ ، وَالجُزْئِيُّ الإِيجَابِيُّ وَالسَّلْبِيُّ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَبِأَنَّ «اللام» لِلْجِنْسِ . وَلَكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: أَرَادَ بـ «الجَمْع»: المُثَنَّى ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي نَظَائِرِه . قَوْلُهُ : (إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ ... إلخ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ إِذْ تَعْلِيلِيَّةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا وَقْتِيَّةٌ. وَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ ، فَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ رَاجِعُ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ، بِتَقْدِيرِ المُضَافِ المُتَقَدِّم، وَالصَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِاِسْمِ الإِشَارَةِ ؛ وَالمَعْنَى : لِأَنَّ هَيْئَةَ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ مَعَ اعْتِبَارِ الْأَسْوَارِ يُشَارُ لَهَا بِالضَّرْبِ .
But what my master Sa'id taught is that the meaning of the author's saying 'without considering the quantifiers' is: without requiring consideration of them. So what is negated is only the condition of considering them. And in that case, it applies both when they are considered and when they are not considered. And upon this, between the figure and the mood there is absolute generality and specificity, because every mood is a figure, but not conversely. And al-Mallawi deemed that unlikely from the author's speech, but it is more appropriate with their saying 'the moods of the figures,' i.e., their types. For if it is said: the syllogism contains only two quantifiers, so why did the author use the plural when he said 'without considering the quantifiers'? It is answered: he used the plural because the quantifier in itself has four divisions: the universal affirmative, the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative, as previously mentioned, and because the 'lām' is for the genus. And you may say: he intended by 'the plural' the dual, as previously in its analogues. His saying: ('since that by the mood ... etc.') It is possible that 'idh' is causal, and possible that it is temporal. On the first possibility, the demonstrative pronoun refers to the mentioned two premises of the syllogism, with an implied genitive construction as before, and the pronoun refers back to the demonstrative pronoun; and the meaning is: because the form of the two premises of the syllogism with consideration of the quantifiers is indicated by the mood.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative interpretation of the condition 'min ghayri an tu'tabara al-aswar' and resolves potential objections. According to Sayyid Sa'id, the phrase does not mean that quantifiers must be absent, but only that they are not required; they may be present or absent. This leads to a relation of 'umum wa khusus mutlaq' (absolute generality and specificity) between figure and mood: every mood is a figure (since a mood already has a figure), but not every figure is a specific mood (because a figure can have multiple moods). Al-Mallawi doubted this reading, but Bajuri finds it more consistent with the common phrase 'dharub al-ashkal' (moods of the figures), where moods are types of figures, implying generality and specificity. The objection is raised: a syllogism has only two quantifiers, so why does the author use the plural 'aswar' (quantifiers)? The answer is that the quantifier itself has four types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), so the plural is justified. Alternatively, the plural may be interpreted as a dual, as is sometimes done. Finally, the passage discusses the phrase 'idh dhaka bi-ad-darb lahu yushar', explaining that 'idh' could be causal or temporal. If causal, it means 'because the form of the two premises with quantifiers is indicated by the mood.' This clarifies that the mood specifically relates to the quantifier-considered form.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl): The form of the two premises of a syllogism, which under this interpretation may or may not include quantifier consideration.
ضرب (darb): A specific type of figure determined by the quantifiers, i.e., a figure with a specified mood.
السور (al-sawr): The quantifier, which has four divisions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
إذ (idh): A particle that can be either causal (meaning 'because') or temporal (meaning 'when') in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mutual exclusivity (tabayun)
B) Identity (inniyya)
C) Absolute generality and specificity (umum wa khusus mutlaq)
D) Equivalence (musawat)

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation establishes that figure and mood are in a relation of absolute generality and specificity, and the plural 'aswar' is justified either by the four types of quantifiers or as a dual.

The Syllogism

Distinguishing Shape and Mood in Syllogism
Author explains the phrase 'without considering quantifiers' and the difference between shape and mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shakl and darb in a categorical syllogism and explain the role of quantifiers in that distinction.

Core Idea
Mood is shape with quantifiers considered; shape is pure configuration.

In Islamic logic, every syllogism has a form defined by its premises. But there are two levels: the bare arrangement of terms (shape) and the arrangement with quantifiers and quality considered (mood). This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam's poem clarifies that relationship, showing that mood is a subtype of shape. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying syllogisms in works like al-Qutb al-Tahtani's commentary on al-Shamsiyya.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two key terms
Find where the passage defines 'shakl' and 'darb'. Note the Arabic terms and their translations. These are foundational for syllogism classification.
The terms appear in the context of explaining 'without considering quantifiers'.
2
Identify the defining condition: quantifiers
The passage repeatedly states that the difference lies in 'considering the quantifiers' (i'tibar al-aswar). Determine what this condition entails: the quantifier specifies quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). Shakl ignores quantifiers; darb includes them.
Quantifiers are crucial for determining the mood of a syllogism.
3
Work out the logical relationship
Al-Bajuri concludes that every darb is a shakl, but not every shakl is a darb (absolute generality). Verify this: since darb is a subset of configurations that meet an extra condition, it must be a subclass.
This is like 'square' vs 'rectangle' – every square is a rectangle due to extra condition (equal sides).
4
Analyze the plural 'aswar' objection
The passage addresses why the author used the plural 'quantifiers' when only two appear per syllogism. Al-Bajuri gives two reasons: the quantifier itself has four divisions, or 'lam' indicates the genus. Internalize this as a subtlety of Arabic usage in technical texts.
This shows careful reading of the matn's wording.
5
Synthesize the final definitions
Combine all insights to produce a crisp distinction: shakl = configuration without quantifier condition; darb = configuration with quantifier condition. Apply to sample syllogisms to confirm you can identify each.
This is the core skill the lesson aims to teach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأشكال (٨٣) مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ لَهُ يُشَارُ الباجوري لَكِنَّ الَّذِي أَفَادَهُ سَيْدِي سَعِيد (١) أَنَّ مَعْنَى قَوْلِ المُصَنِّفِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُه : مِنْ غَيْرِ اشْتِرَاطِ اعْتِبَارِهَا ، فَالمَنْفِيُّ (٢) إِنَّمَا هُوَ شَرْط (۳) اعْتِبَارِهَا ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَصْدُقُ بِاعْتِبَارِهَا وَعَدَمِ اعْتِبَارِهَا ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَبَيْنَ الشَّكْلِ وَالضَّرْبِ : «العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ  ِلأَنَّ كُلَّ ضَرْبٍ شَكُلِّ، وَلَا عَكْسُ، وَاسْتَبْعَدَ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ ذَلِكَ مِنْ كَلامِ المُصَنِّفِ (٤) ، لَكِنَّهُ هُوَ الأَنْسَبُ بِقَوْلِهِمْ: ضُرُوبُ الأَشْكَالِ»؛ أَيْ (٥) : أَنْوَاعُهَا . فَإِنْ قِيلَ : القِيَاسُ لَا يَشْتَمِلُ إِلَّا عَلَى سُورَيْنِ ، فَلِمَ جَمَعَ المُصَنِّفُ حَيْثُ قَالَ: مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ تُعْتَبَرَ الأَسْوَارُ»؟ أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ جَمَعَ؛ نَظَراً إِلَى أَنَّ السُّورَ فِي حَدٌ ذَاتِهِ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَام : السُّورُ الكُلِّيُّ الإِيجَابِيُّ وَالسَّلْبِيُّ ، وَالجُزْئِيُّ (٦) الإِيجَابِيُّ وَالسَّلْبِيُّ ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، وَبِأَنَّ «اللام» لِلْجِنْسِ . وَلَكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: أَرَادَ بـ «الجَمْع»: المُثَنَّى ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي نَظَائِرِه . قَوْلُهُ : (إِذْ ذَاكَ بِالضَّرْبِ ... إلخ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ إِذْ تَعْلِيلِيَّةٌ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا وَقْتِيَّةٌ. وَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ ، فَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ رَاجِعُ لِلمَذْكُورِ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَي القِيَاسِ، بِتَقْدِيرِ المُضَافِ المُتَقَدِّم، وَالصَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِاِسْمِ الإِشَارَةِ ؛ وَالمَعْنَى : لِأَنَّ هَيْئَةَ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ مَعَ اعْتِبَارِ الْأَسْوَارِ يُشَارُ لَهَا بِالضَّرْبِ . وَعَلَى الثَّانِي، فَاسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ رَاجِعُ لِاعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ، وَالضَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ لِلمَذْكُورٍ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَي القِيَاسِ، بِتَقْدِيرِ المُضَافِ السَّابِقِ ، وَالمَعْنَى : وَقْتَ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ يُشَارُ لِهَيْئَةِ قَضِيَّتَي القِيَاسِ بِالضَّرْبِ . وَالمُرَادُ بِالإِشَارَةِ هُنَا : الدَّلَالَةُ، فَاللَّامُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : «لَهُ) بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى، كَمَا يُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ كَلَامِ الشَّيْخ المَلوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ ) . وَالحَاصِلُ : أَنَّ الضَّرْبَ اسْمٌ لِهَيْئَةِ قَضِيَّتَيِ القِيَاسِ الحَاصِلَةِ مِنِ اجْتِمَاعِ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ الكُبْرَى باعْتِبَارِ طَرَفَيِ المَطْلُوبِ مَعَ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ بِشَرْطَ اعْتِبَارِ الأَسْوَارِ ؛ كَأَنْ يُلَاحَظَ كَوْنَ هَاتَيْنِ القَضِيَّتَيْنِ كُلِّيتَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ الشَّكْلِ ، فَإِنَّهُ اسْمٌ لِلْهَيِّئَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ لَا بِهَذَا الشَّرْطِ، بَلْ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ اعْتِبَارِ الْأَسْوَارِ، أَوْ بِلَا شَرْطٍ عَلَى مَا تَقَدَّمَ، فَلَا تَغْفَلْ.
Chapter on the figures. (83) 'Without considering the quantifiers, because at that time by 'mood' it is pointed to.' Al-Bajuri: But that which my master Sa'id conveyed is that the meaning of the author's statement 'without considering the quantifiers' is: without stipulating their consideration. So what is negated is only the condition of considering them. In that case, it applies both with consideration and without consideration. Therefore, between shape and mood there is absolute generality and specificity, because every mood is a shape, but not every shape is a mood. Al-Malawi found this unlikely from the author's words, but it is the most suitable given their expression 'moods of the shapes' meaning its types. If it is said: the syllogism includes only two quantifiers, so why did the author use the plural in 'without considering the quantifiers'? The answer: he used the plural considering that the quantifier in itself has four divisions: universal affirmative and negative, and particular affirmative and negative, as previously mentioned, and because the 'lam' is for the genus. You may also say: by 'plural' he meant the dual, as previously mentioned in similar contexts. His statement: 'because at that time by mood...' etc. It is possible that 'idh' is causative, and possible that it is temporal. According to the first, the demonstrative pronoun refers to the mentioned two premises of the syllogism, with the implied preceding genitive construction, and the pronoun refers to the demonstrative; the meaning: because the configuration of the two premises of the syllogism with consideration of the quantifiers is pointed to by 'mood'. According to the second, the demonstrative refers to the consideration of the quantifiers, and the pronoun refers to the mentioned two premises, with the previous implied genitive; the meaning: at the time of considering the quantifiers, the configuration of the two premises of the syllogism is pointed to by 'mood'. What is meant by 'pointing' here is indication; so the 'lam' in 'lahu' means 'ala, as understood from the words of al-Malawi in his large commentary. The conclusion: mood is a name for the configuration of the two premises of the syllogism resulting from the combination of the minor and major with respect to the extremes of the conclusion and the middle term, with the condition of considering the quantifiers, e.g., observing that both premises are universal; unlike shape, which is a name for the mentioned configuration without this condition, but with the condition of not considering the quantifiers, or without any condition as previously discussed. So do not neglect.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from al-Sullam's poem, defining the technical terms 'shape' (shakl) and 'mood' (darb) in syllogistic logic. The commentator, al-Bajuri, begins by clarifying the author's phrase 'without considering the quantifiers.' He reports that his teacher, Sayyid Sa'id, interpreted this as meaning that the condition of considering quantifiers is negated, but the actual consideration is not negated. Thus, the term 'shape' applies both when quantifiers are considered and when they are not. From this, a relationship of absolute generality and specificity emerges: every mood is a shape, but not every shape is a mood. Al-Bajuri notes that al-Malawi found this interpretation unlikely, but he defends it as consistent with the common phrase 'moods of the shapes,' where 'moods' are the types of shapes. Next, an objection is raised: a syllogism contains only two quantifiers (one for each premise), so why did the author use the plural 'quantifiers'? The answer is twofold: first, the quantifier in itself has four divisions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative); second, the 'lam' in 'aswar' indicates the genus, so the plural can refer to the genus. Alternatively, one can say that the author meant the dual by the plural form 'aswar', as is customary in Arabic. The commentary then examines the word 'idh' in the matn line: it can be causative or temporal. If causative, the phrase means: 'because the configuration of the premises with quantifiers is called mood'; if temporal, it means: 'at the time of considering quantifiers, the configuration is called mood.' After explaining both, al-Bajuri summarizes that 'mood' is the name for the configuration of the premises that considers quantifiers, while 'shape' is the same configuration without that condition (or with the condition of ignoring quantifiers). This distinction is crucial for understanding the classification of syllogisms.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl): shape
ضرب (darb): mood
سور (sawr): quantifier

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the relationship between shape and mood?
A) Every shape is a mood, and every mood is a shape.
B) Every mood is a shape, but not every shape is a mood.
C) Shape and mood are completely independent classifications.
D) Mood is a type of shape only when quantifiers are ignored.

Key Takeaway
Mood is a subtype of shape defined by the presence of quantifier consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.' Identify its shape and its mood, explaining how quantifiers determine the mood.
?
Consider the two premises: 'Some scholars are logicians' and 'All logicians are precise.' Can this form a syllogism? What shape would it have? What mood? If the conclusion is 'Some scholars are precise,' is the mood valid? (Refer to the conditions of the first figure.)

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying the Four Figures of the Syllogism by Middle Term Position
Author defines the four figures of categorical syllogism based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of any categorical syllogism by locating the middle term's position in each premise.

Core Idea
The figure depends solely on whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise.

The categorical syllogism can take one of four forms, called figures (ashkal). These figures are distinguished not by the terms themselves but by the arrangement of the middle term in the two premises. Understanding this classification is the first step toward evaluating a syllogism's validity. In this lesson, we will learn how to identify each figure using the middle term's position.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
In a syllogism like 'Every intoxicant is prohibited; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is prohibited', the middle term is 'intoxicant'. Find the term that occurs in both premises but not in the conclusion.
The middle term links the minor and major terms.
2
Identify the position in the minor premise
The minor premise is the one that contains the subject of the conclusion. In our example, 'khamr is an intoxicant' is the minor premise. Determine whether the middle term ('intoxicant') is a predicate (what is said about the subject) or a subject (the thing being described). Here it is the predicate.
If the middle term follows 'is' or another copula, it is usually the predicate.
3
Identify the position in the major premise
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. In our example, 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' is the major premise. Here the middle term ('intoxicant') is the subject (the thing being described).
The major premise often starts with 'every' or 'no' and the middle term is the first noun.
4
Match the pair to one of the four figures
Using the combinations: (1) predicate in minor, subject in major = first figure; (2) predicate in both = second; (3) subject in both = third; (4) subject in minor, predicate in major = fourth. Our example is first figure.
The passage only explicitly names the first three, but the fourth is implied by the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٨٤) وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط : (٨٥) حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِـ: «شَكْلِ أَوَّلِ وَيُدْرَى (٨٦) وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِف الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : (وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ) المُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ المُثَنَّى ، كَمَا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَقَط) مُقَدَّمٌ مِنْ تَأْخِيرِ ؛ لِأَنَّ حَقَّهَا التَّأْخِيرُ عَنْ قَوْلِهِ : «أَرْبَعَةٌ»، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى قَوْلُهُ : (بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط) أَيْ : بِالنَّظرِ لِأَحْوَالِهِ مِنْ حَمْلِهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَوَضْعِهِ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَحَمْلِهِ فِيهِمَا ، وَوَضْعِهِ فِيهِمَا ، وَوَضْعِهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَحَمْلِهِ فِي الكُبْرَى؛ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا بَعْدُ. قَوْلُهُ : (حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى ... إلخ) أَيْ: كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلٌّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْم، وَهَذَا وَمَا بَعْدَهُ تَفْصِيلُ وَبَيَانُ لِمَا قَبْلَهُ . قَوْلُهُ : (يُدْعَى بِـ : شَكْلٍ أَوَّلِ) أَيْ : يُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ . وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي ذَلِكَ مِنَ التَّسَامُحِ ؛ لِأَنَّ ظَاهِرَهُ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بـ «الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : المَذْكُورُ مِنَ الحَمْلِ وَالوَضْعِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الهَيْئَةُ الحَاصِلَةُ بِسَبَبٍ ذَلِكَ، وَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِيمَا بَعْدُ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَيُدْرَى أَيْ : بِشَكْلِ أَوَّلِ، فَفِيهِ الحَذْفُ مِنَ الثَّانِي لِدَلَالَةِ الأَوَّلِ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ أَيْ : كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَان . (1)
(84) 'And for the premises there are only four figures, according to the middle term: (85) predicated in the minor and subject in the major is called the first figure, and it is known; (86) and its being predicated in both is known as the second, and its being subject in both is known as the third.' Al-Bajuri: His statement 'and for the premises' – the plural here is intended as dual, as previously. His statement 'only' is fronted from its proper place, because it should properly come after 'four', as is obvious. His statement 'according to the middle term' means: with regard to its states: that it is predicated in the minor and subject in the major, predicated in both, subject in both, and subject in the minor and predicated in the major – as will be understood from what follows. His statement 'predicated in the minor ... etc.' means: as in your statement: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body.' This and what follows are a detailed explanation of what preceded. His statement 'is called the first figure' means: it is named that. And what is in this is not free from looseness, because its apparent meaning is that what is named 'first figure' is the mentioned predication and subjection, whereas what is named by it is only the configuration resulting from that. And the same applies to what follows. His statement 'and it is known' means: by the first figure, so there is ellipsis in the second due to the indication of the first. His statement 'and its being predicated in both' means: as in your statement: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from stone is an animal.' (1)
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term (hadd wasat) in the two premises. The commentator, al-Bajuri, first notes that the plural 'premises' actually refers to the dual (the two premises), as is common. He also observes that the word 'only' (faqat) is placed before 'four' for emphasis but logically belongs after it. The key criterion for the figures is the middle term's relation: whether it is predicated (haml, i.e., serves as predicate) or subject (wad', i.e., serves as subject) in each premise. The four possible combinations are enumerated: (1) middle term predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major – this is the first figure; (2) predicate in both premises – second figure; (3) subject in both – third figure; (4) subject in the minor and predicate in the major – fourth figure. The matn lines explicitly mention only the first three, but the commentary includes the fourth in the preceding explanation. For the first figure, the example given is the classic: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body' (which yields 'Every human is a body'). The second figure example is: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from stone is an animal' (yielding 'Nothing from stone is a human'). The commentator notes a looseness in the matn: the words 'predicated in the minor, subject in the major' are said to be 'called the first figure', but strictly speaking, the configuration resulting from that arrangement is the figure, not the arrangement itself. This nuance applies to all figures.

Key Terms
حد وسط
شكل أول
حمل
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
D) Subject in the minor and predicate in the major.

Key Takeaway
The figure of a categorical syllogism is determined solely by the middle term's position as subject or predicate in each premise, yielding four possible configurations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Bajuri enumerates and explains the four figures defined by middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of any categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term in each premise.

The figure of a categorical syllogism is its shape—how the middle term is placed in the two premises. There are exactly four possible arrangements, and knowing them is essential for evaluating syllogistic validity. In this lesson, we will explore each figure with examples from Islamic logic, so you can recognize them in any argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term (al-hadd al-wasaṭ)
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For example, in 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body', the middle term is 'animal'.
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major premise contains the predicate.
2
Check the position of the middle term in the minor premise
Is it the subject (subject-position) or the predicate (predication)? In the minor premise 'Every human is an animal', the middle term 'animal' is the predicate.
3
Check the position of the middle term in the major premise
In the major premise 'Every animal is a body', the middle term 'animal' is the subject. So in this case: predicate in minor, subject in major.
4
Match to the figure definition
Use Bajuri's classification: first figure (predicate in minor, subject in major), second figure (predicate in both), third figure (subject in both), fourth figure (subject in minor, predicate in major). The example is first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَشْكَالٌ فَقَط أَرْبَعَةٌ بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط : (٨٥) حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى وَضْعُهُ بِكُبْرَى يُدْعَى بِـ: «شَكْلِ أَوَّلِ وَيُدْرَى (٨٦) وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ: «ثَالِثاً» أُلِف (۸۷) وَ رَابِعُ الأَشْكَالِ : عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَلِلْمُقَدِّمَاتِ) المُرَادُ بِالجَمْعِ المُثَنَّى ، كَمَا مَرَّ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَقَط) مُقَدَّمٌ مِنْ تَأْخِيرِ ؛ لِأَنَّ حَقَّهَا التَّأْخِيرُ عَنْ قَوْلِهِ : «أَرْبَعَةٌ»، كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى قَوْلُهُ : (بِحَسَبِ الحَدِّ الوَسَط) أَيْ : بِالنَّظرِ لِأَحْوَالِهِ مِنْ حَمْلِهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَوَضْعِهِ فِي الكُبْرَى، وَحَمْلِهِ فِيهِمَا ، وَوَضْعِهِ فِيهِمَا ، وَوَضْعِهِ فِي الصُّغْرَى وَحَمْلِهِ فِي الكُبْرَى؛ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا بَعْدُ. قَوْلُهُ : (حَمْلٌ بِصُغْرَى ... إلخ) أَيْ: كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلٌّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْم، وَهَذَا وَمَا بَعْدَهُ تَفْصِيلُ وَبَيَانُ لِمَا قَبْلَهُ . قَوْلُهُ : (يُدْعَى بِـ : شَكْلٍ أَوَّلِ) أَيْ : يُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ . وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي ذَلِكَ مِنَ التَّسَامُحِ ؛ لِأَنَّ ظَاهِرَهُ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بـ «الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : المَذْكُورُ مِنَ الحَمْلِ وَالوَضْعِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بِهِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الهَيْئَةُ الحَاصِلَةُ بِسَبَبٍ ذَلِكَ، وَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِيمَا بَعْدُ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَيُدْرَى أَيْ : بِشَكْلِ أَوَّلِ، فَفِيهِ الحَذْفُ مِنَ الثَّانِي لِدَلَالَةِ الأَوَّلِ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَحَمْلُهُ فِي الكُلِّ أَيْ : كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَان . قَوْلُهُ : ( «ثَانِياً» عُرِفْ أَيْ: عُرِفَ شَكلاً ثَانِياً ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ، فَيَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ ضَمَّنَ عُرِفْ مَعْنَى : سُمِّيَ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَوَضْعُهُ فِي الكُلِّ أَيْ: كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَسَّاسَ، وَبَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ . قَوْلُهُ : ثَالِثَاً أُلِفْ أَيْ : أَلِف شَكلاً ثَالِثَاً ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ، فَيَكُونُ قَدْ دَخَلَهُ التَّضْمِينُ، كَمَا مَرَّ فِي نَظِيرِهِ. قَوْلُهُ : (عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ) أَيْ : وَضْعُهُ بِالصُّغْرَى وَحَمْلُهُ بِالكُبْرَى ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ، وَبَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانُ.
And for the premises there are only four figures according to the middle term: (85) Predication in the minor and its subject-position in the major is called 'first figure' and is known; (86) and its predication in both is known as 'second', and its subject-position in both is composed as 'third'; (87) and the fourth figure is the reverse of the first. His statement: 'And for the premises' — what is meant by the plural is the dual, as before. His statement: 'only' is fronted from its proper place, for it should be after 'four', as is obvious. His statement: 'according to the middle term' — i.e., considering its states: its being predicate in the minor and subject in the major, its being predicate in both, its being subject in both, and its being subject in the minor and predicate in the major, as is known from what follows. His statement: 'Predication in the minor... etc.' — i.e., as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and every animal is a body.' This and what follows is a detailing and clarification of what preceded. His statement: 'is called first figure' — i.e., it is named thus. And it is not hidden that there is looseness in that, because its apparent meaning is that what is named 'first figure' is the mentioned predication and subject-position, whereas what is named thereby is only the form resulting from that. And similarly it is said in what follows. His statement: 'and is known' — i.e., by first figure; so there is ellipsis in the second due to the indication of the first. His statement: 'and its predication in both' — i.e., as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from stone is an animal.' His statement: 'known as second' — i.e., it is known as a second figure, meaning it is named thus; so the author has embedded in 'known' the meaning 'named'. His statement: 'and its subject-position in both' — i.e., as in your saying: 'Every animal is sentient, and some animal is rational.' His statement: 'composed as third' — i.e., it is composed as a third figure, meaning it is named thus; so it has incorporated [the meaning], as previously in its counterpart. His statement: 'reverse of the first' — i.e., its subject-position in the minor and its predication in the major; as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some rational is a human.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and enumerative passage. It presents the four figures of the categorical syllogism, defined by the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-wasat). The matn states that there are exactly four figures. The first figure occurs when the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise. The second figure is when the middle term is predicate in both premises. The third figure is when the middle term is subject in both premises. The fourth figure is the reverse of the first, i.e., subject in the minor and predicate in the major. Bajuri's commentary clarifies each point: the plural 'premises' here means the two premises; the word 'only' is fronted for emphasis; 'according to the middle term' refers to its four possible positions. For the first figure, he gives the example 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body.' He notes a looseness: the matn's wording might suggest that the predication/subject arrangement itself is called the figure, but actually the figure is the form resulting from that arrangement. For the second figure, the example is 'Every human is an animal, nothing from stone is an animal' (middle term 'animal' is predicate in both). For the third figure, the example is 'Every animal is sentient, some animal is rational' (middle term 'animal' is subject in both). For the fourth figure, the example is 'Every human is an animal, some rational is a human' (middle term 'human' is subject in the minor and predicate in the major). This lesson gives a complete enumeration of the four figures with their characteristic forms and examples.

الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
الحد الوسط
