Productive Moods of Each Figure
Author enumerates productive moods per figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the number of productive moods in each figure of the categorical syllogism and compute the total number of productive and non-productive moods.

Core Idea
First and second figures have 4 productive moods each, third has 6, fourth has 5.

Al-Bajuri explains how many moods in each figure yield a necessary conclusion. Knowing these numbers helps you quickly assess a syllogism's validity potential and avoid wasting time on unproductive forms.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the counts
From the matn: first figure = 4, second = 4, third = 6, fourth = 5 (ancient view). Note that the third figure has the most because its middle term position allows more combinations.
These are the productive moods; all others are non-productive.
2
Understand total moods
Each figure has 4 possible premise pairs per quantity/quality? Actually there are 4×4=16 premise combinations per figure, but here the total possible moods across all figures is 64 (4 figures × 16 premise combinations). The author counts productive moods as 19, leaving 45 non-productive.
Later scholars revised productive to 22, so non-productive = 42.
3
Apply to an example
Take a first-figure syllogism: 'All intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' This is a productive mood (Barbara). If the premises were 'Some intoxicants are forbidden; some drinks are intoxicants', that would not yield a necessary conclusion – it is non-productive.
Recognize that not every combination works; only the listed moods are reliable.
4
Compute non-productive total
Total moods 64 minus productive 19 = 45 non-productive. Verify: 4+4+6+5=19. So non-productive = 64-19=45.
The later view gives 64-22=42.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمُنْتِجُ لِأَوَّلِ: أَرْبَعَةُ، كَالثَّانِ، ثُمَّ ثَالِتٌ: سِتَّةُ، وَرَابِعُ: بِخَمْسَةٍ قَدْ أَنْتَجَا. وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا. وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ.
قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ «الثَّانِ) أَيْ : فِي أَنَّ الْمُنْتِجَ لَهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ.
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ ثَالِتٌ») يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الذِّكْرِ ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الشَّرَفِ؛ لأَنَّ الشَّكْلَيْنِ الأَوَّلَيْنِ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الآخَرَيْنِ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (فَسِتَّةُ) أَيْ : فَالمُنْتِجُ لَهُ سِنَةٌ ، فَقَوْلُهُ : سِتَّةُ) خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ، وَالفَاءُ زَائِدَةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَرَابِعُ : بِخَمْسَةٍ ... إلخ) الجَارُّ وَالمَجْرُورُ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِقَوْلِهِ : «قَدْ أَنْتَجَا»، و«البَاءُ» بِمَعْنَى : في» ؛ وَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّ الشَّكْلَ الرَّابِعَ قَدْ أَنْتَجَ فِي خَمْسَةِ أَضْرُبٍ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ هَذَا مَذْهَبُ الْأَقْدَمِينَ، فَتَنَبَّهُ . قوْلُهُ : (وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا) وَجُمْلَتُهُ : خَمْسَةٌ وَأَرْبَعُونَ ضَرْباً ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ أَنَّ المُنْتِجَ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الرّابع خَمْسَةٌ فَقَطْ ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ الضُّرُوبَ العَفْلِيَّةَ بِاعْتِبَارِ جَمِيعِ الْأَشْكَالِ أَرْبَعَةٌ وَسِتُّونَ، فَإِذَا أَسْقَطْتَ المُنْتِجَ وَهُوَ تِسْعَةَ عَشَرَ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا مَرَّ ، بَقِيَ خَمْسَةٌ وَأَرْبَعُونَ ، وَأَمَّا عَلَى مَا ذَهَبَ إِلَيْهِ بَعْضُ المُتَأخّرِينَ فَ : اثْنَانِ وَأَرْبَعُونَ ؛ لأَنَّ المُنْتِجَ عِنْدَهُمْ اثْنَانِ وَعِشْرُونَ ، فَإِذَا أَسْقَطْتَهُ بَقِيَ اثْنَانِ وَأَرْبَعُونَ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ كَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يَقُولَ: «الخِسَا»؛ لِأَنَّ المَوْصُوفَ بِذَلِكَ المُقَدِّمَهُ ، وَهِيَ مُؤَنَّتَةٌ ، لَكِنَّهُ ذَكَرَ بِاعْتِبَارِ تَأْوِيلِ المُقَدِّمَةِ بِالقَوْلِ. قَالَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : وَيُمْكِنُ أَنَّ التَّذْكِيرَ لِوُقُوعِ الأَخَسَ عَلَى الكَمْ أَوِ الكَيْفِ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ «أَفْعُلَ» التَّفْضِيلِ لَيْسَ عَلَى بَابِهِ ؛ إِذِ الخِرِّيَّةُ لَيْسَتْ مُتَحَقِّقَةٌ فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، فَهُوَ بِمَعْنَى الخَسِيسِ . قَوْلُهُ : (هَكَذَا زُكِنْ) أَيْ : فُهِمَ وَعُلِمَ هَكَذَا .
The productive of the first [figure] is four, like the second, then the third is six, and the fourth has produced five. And other than what I have mentioned will not produce. And the conclusion follows the inferior of those premises; thus it is known.
His statement: 'like the second' means that the productive for it is four.
His statement: 'then the third' – 'then' may be for ordering in mention, or for ordering in honor, because the first two figures are more honorable than the other two, as known from what preceded.
His statement: 'six' – meaning the productive for it is six, so his statement 'six' is a predicate of an omitted subject, and the fa' is extra. His statement: 'and the fourth with five ... etc.' – the preposition and its object are attached to his statement 'has produced', and 'with' means 'in'; the meaning is that the fourth figure has produced in five moods, and you have known that this is the doctrine of the ancients, so be alert. His statement: 'and other than what I have mentioned will not produce' – its total is forty-five moods, based on the author's statement that the productive from the fourth figure is only five, because the actual moods considering all figures are sixty-four, and when you subtract the productive which is nineteen based on what preceded, there remains forty-five. According to what some later scholars held, [the non-productive] is forty-two, because according to them the productive is twenty-two, so when you subtract it, forty-two remain. His statement: 'and the conclusion follows the inferior' – the apparent requirement was to say 'the inferior ones' because the described thing is the premise, which is feminine, but he mentioned considering the interpretation of the premise as a statement. Some investigators said: the masculine form may be because 'inferior' occurs on quantity or quality. It is not hidden that the elative 'af'al' is not used properly, since inferiority is not actually realized in each of the two sides, so it means 'low'. His statement: 'thus it is known' – i.e., thus it is understood and known.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage defines and counts the productive moods of each figure in a categorical syllogism. The matn states: the first figure has 4 productive moods, the second also 4, the third has 6, and the fourth has 5. Any mood not mentioned is non-productive. Additionally, the conclusion follows the inferior (less universal or less certain) of the two premises. The commentary by al-Bajuri expands on each phrase. The word 'like the second' is explained to mean the second figure also has 4 productive moods. The word 'then' in 'then the third' is discussed whether it indicates order of mention or order of honor; the first two figures are more honorable. For 'six', it is the predicate of an omitted subject. For 'with five', the preposition 'with' means 'in', meaning the fourth figure produces in five moods, and this is the doctrine of the ancients. The total non-productive moods across all figures is 45 according to the author (since total moods are 64, productive 19, leaving 45). Later scholars consider productive moods to be 22, so non-productive is 42. The statement that the conclusion follows the inferior premise is explained grammatically: 'inferior' is masculine because it is considered as a statement, or it refers to quantity/quality. The elative is used in a non-standard sense since there may not be actual inequality in both sides; it simply means 'low' or 'lesser'. The final phrase 'hākadhā zukina' means 'thus it is known'.

شكل
ضرب
نتيجة
أخس

Checkpoint
According to the matn, how many productive moods does the fourth figure have?
A) 4
B) 5
C) 6
D) 19

Key Takeaway
The first figure has 4 productive moods, second 4, third 6, fourth 5, totaling 19 productive moods out of 64 possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the number of productive moods for each of the four figures according to the matn. Then compute the total number of possible moods across all figures (64) and verify that the number of non-productive moods is 45.
?
Given a syllogism in the third figure with premises 'All A are B' and 'All C are B', determine whether it falls into a productive mood. (Hint: The third figure has six productive moods, but not all combinations work; check if the conclusion must follow necessarily.)

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Rule of the Inferior Premise
Author explains the rule that conclusion follows the inferior premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the conclusion of a syllogism must follow the inferior (less universal or less certain) premise.

Core Idea
The conclusion cannot be stronger than its weakest premise.

Al-Bajuri explains a crucial principle: the conclusion's quantity and modality are determined by the 'inferior' premise. This rule ensures that syllogistic reasoning stays sound and prevents overreaching conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Define 'inferior' premise
The 'inferior' (akhass) premise is the one that is less universal (e.g., particular instead of universal) or less certain (e.g., possible instead of necessary). It sets the limit for the conclusion.
In a valid syllogism, the conclusion must match the inferior premise in universality and modality.
2
Identify the inferior premise in an example
Take: 'All scholars are respected (universal affirmative)' and 'Some students are scholars (particular affirmative)'. The inferior premise is the second because it is particular. Therefore the conclusion must be particular: 'Some students are respected'.
If we concluded 'All students are respected', that would be invalid.
3
Apply to modality
If one premise is necessary (e.g., 'Every human is a rational animal') and the other is possible (e.g., 'Some beings are human'), the conclusion must be possible, not necessary.
This prevents claiming necessity from possibility.
4
Recognize grammatical nuance
Al-Bajuri notes that the author used 'akhass' (masculine) rather than 'khusra' (feminine) because 'inferior' refers to the premise qua statement (qawl), not the premise itself. This is a subtle point but shows the care taken in formulation.
Not essential for application, but helps understand the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمُنْتِجُ لِأَوَّلِ: أَرْبَعَةُ، كَالثَّانِ، ثُمَّ ثَالِتٌ: سِتَّةُ، وَرَابِعُ: بِخَمْسَةٍ قَدْ أَنْتَجَا. وَغَيْرُ مَا ذَكَرْتُهُ لَنْ يُنْتِجَا. وَتَتْبَعُ النَّتِيجَةُ الأَخَسَّ مِنْ تِلْكَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ هَكَذَا زُكِنْ.
قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ «الثَّانِ) أَيْ : فِي أَنَّ الْمُنْتِجَ لَهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ.
قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ ثَالِتٌ») يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الذِّكْرِ ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الشَّرَفِ؛ لأَنَّ الشَّكْلَيْنِ الأَوَّلَيْنِ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الآخَرَيْنِ، كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ .

If one premise is universal and the other is particular, which premise is considered inferior?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Do Figures Apply to Conditional Syllogisms?
Author asserts figures specific to categorical; commentator gives preferred view including conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures in a conditional syllogism by analyzing the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
Figures are defined by middle term position, not premise type.

In this passage, the matn author asserts that the four figures of syllogisms are limited to categorical syllogisms. However, the commentator, al-Bajuri, presents a more comprehensive view: the figures apply to conditional syllogisms as well. The key insight is that the definition of a figure depends solely on where the middle term appears—as antecedent or consequent in each premise—not on whether the premises are categorical or conditional. This lesson will help you understand this expansion and apply it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the matn's claim
Find the sentence 'وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِيِّ مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِيِّ' in the source. This is the author's position: figures are restricted to categorical syllogisms.
The 'ba' in 'bil-ḥamlī' indicates restriction.
2
Understand the commentary's clarification
Al-Bajuri explains the matn's wording, noting the intended restriction, but then introduces the preferred view (al-rājiḥ) that figures are not exclusive to categoricals.
He says 'وَالرَّاجِحُ أَنَّهَا لَا تَخْتَصُّ بِالحَمْلِي'.
3
Extract the definition of the four figures
Al-Bajuri defines each figure by the position of the middle term: first figure (consequent in minor, antecedent in major), second (consequent in both), third (antecedent in both), fourth (antecedent in minor, consequent in major).
This definition does not mention categorical or conditional—it is purely about position.
4
Apply to a conditional syllogism
Take a conditional syllogism with two conditional premises, e.g., 'If A then B; If B then C; therefore If A then C'. The middle term B is consequent in the first premise and antecedent in the second, matching the first figure. This confirms figures apply to conditionals.
The passage gives a similar example with 'sun rising' and 'daytime exists'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِيِّ مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِيّ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ ... إلخ) «البَاءُ » دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ عَلَيْهِ ، فَالمَعْنَى : أَنَّ الْأَشْكَالَ الأَرْبَعَةَ المَذْكُورَةَ مَقْصُورَةٌ عَلَى الحَمْلِي ، وَلَا تَتَعَدَّاهُ إِلَى الشَّرْطِيّ ، وَهَذِهِ طَريقَةٌ لِلمُصَنِّف .
وَالرَّاجِحُ أَنَّهَا لَا تَخْتَصُّ بِالحَمْلِي ، بَلْ تَكُونُ فِي الشَّرْطِيِّ أَيْضاً؛ لِأَنَّ :
جَعْلَ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ تَالِياً فِى الصُّغُرَى مُقَدَّماً فِى الكُبْرَى يُسَمَّى : شَكْلاً أَوَّلَ .
وَجَعْلَهُ تَالِياً فِيهِمَا يُسَمَّى : «شَكلاً ثَانِياً».
وَجَعْلَهُ مُقَدَّماً فِيهِمَا يُسَمَّى: «شَكلاً ثَالِثاً».
وَجَعْلَهُ مُقَدَّماً فِي الصُّغْرَى تَالِياً فِي الكُبْرَى يُسَمَّى : (شَكلاً رابعاً». 
فَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُوداً فَاللَّيْلُ حَاصِلٌ، وَعَلَى هَذَا القِيَاسُ .
قَوْلُهُ : ( بالحَمْلِيّ) أَيْ: بِالقِيَاسِ الحَمْلِي .
وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ - كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ - : بِالقَضِيَّةِ الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَذْكِيرُ المُصَنِّفِ لِتَأْوِيلِ القَضِيَّةِ بِالقَوْلِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي) هَذَا تَصْرِيحُ بِمَا عُلِمَ .
وَكَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ أَنْ يَقُولَ : وَلَيْسَتْ بِنَاءِ الثَّانِيثِ ; لِأَنَّ الصَّمِيرَ لِـ «الأَشْكَالِ»، لَكِنَّ المُصَنِّفَ نَظَرَ لِلتَّأْوِيلِ بِالمَذْكُورِ، كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ، وَالمَعْنَى : وَلَيْسَتْ هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ مُلْتَبِسَةٌ بِالقِيَاسِ الشَّرْطِيّ ، أَوْ بِالقَضِيَّةِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، عَلَى نَظِيرِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ.
And these figures are specific to the categorical, not to the conditional.
His statement: 'And these figures... etc.' – the 'ba'' enters upon the restricted, so the meaning is that the four mentioned figures are restricted to the categorical, and do not extend to the conditional, and this is the author's way.
And the preferable [view] is that they are not specific to the categorical, but rather occur in the conditional as well, because:
Making the middle term the consequent in the minor and the antecedent in the major is called the first figure.
And making it the consequent in both is called the second figure.
And making it the antecedent in both is called the third figure.
And making it the antecedent in the minor and the consequent in the major is called the fourth figure.
An example of the first is when you say: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but not every time daytime exists, is the night present, and so on.
His statement: 'to the categorical' – i.e., to the categorical syllogism.
And it is possible that what is intended – as indicated by al-Mallawi – is the categorical proposition, and accordingly the author's masculine form is because he interprets the proposition as a statement.
His statement: 'not to the conditional' – this is an explicit statement of what is known.
And the apparent requirement was that he say 'laysat' (feminine) building on the feminine, because the pronoun refers to 'al-ashkāl' (masculine plural), but the author considered the interpretation [of forms as figures], as indicated by al-Mallawi. The meaning is: these figures are not connected to the conditional syllogism, or to the conditional proposition, analogous to what preceded.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage clarifies whether the four figures of the syllogism apply only to categorical syllogisms or also to conditional ones. The author of the matn states they are specific to categoricals. Al-Bajuri's commentary explains that the 'ba' in 'bil-ḥamlī' indicates restriction: the figures are limited to categorical syllogisms and do not extend to conditionals. However, the commentator then gives the preferred view (al-rājiḥ) that the figures are not exclusive to categoricals but also apply to conditionals. The reason is that the definition of a figure is based entirely on the position of the middle term (whether it is antecedent or consequent in the premises), not on the type of proposition. Thus, if we have a conditional syllogism, we can still identify the figure by the position of the middle term. Al-Bajuri provides the definitions: first figure (middle term consequent in minor, antecedent in major), second (middle term consequent in both), third (middle term antecedent in both), fourth (middle term antecedent in minor, consequent in major). He gives an example for the first figure with a conditional: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; but not every time daytime exists, is the night present.' This is a conditional syllogism in the first figure. The commentary also discusses the grammatical gender: the author used masculine 'laysa' instead of feminine 'laysat' because he considered the phrase as a 'qawl' (statement). Al-Mallawi is cited for this interpretation. The passage thus presents both the author's assertion and the preferred, more inclusive view.

Key Terms
قياس حملي
قياس شرطي
حد وسط
شكل

D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term, not by whether the premises are categorical or conditional.

Permissibility of Deleting Premises or Conclusion
The author states a rule for deleting parts of a syllogism, and the commentator refines its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which deletion of a premise or the conclusion is permissible in a syllogism according to al-Bajuri's commentary.

Core Idea
Deletion is allowed only for one premise or the conclusion, not all premises.

In constructing syllogisms, sometimes we omit a premise or the conclusion for brevity, assuming the missing part is understood. But is this always allowed? The matn and its commentaries set strict conditions. This lesson unpacks those conditions and the scholarly debate around them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule in the matn
Read the matn: 'وَالْحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمٍ آتٍ' – deletion in some premises or the conclusion is for knowledge coming. Note that 'some premises' is ambiguous.
2
Clarify 'some premises' from the commentary
Al-Bajuri explains that 'in some premises' means 'for some of them' (فِي with meaning of لام). 'Some of them' means one of them (minor or major), not a part of a single premise. Understand the restriction: only one premise may be deleted, not a fragment.
3
Understand the condition of maqām istidlāl
Al-Bajuri deduces that deletion of all premises is not allowed, and this holds when the context is inferential reasoning (maqām istidlāl). However, al-‘Attar (via al-Anbabi) challenges this, saying that every context is either rhetorical or inferential, so the condition adds nothing meaningful.
Consider whether the condition is redundant or necessary.
4
Apply the rule to a real syllogism
Take the standard categorical syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' If you delete the minor premise, it is permissible if the listener knows it. If you delete both premises, it is not permissible in a demonstrative context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمٍ آتِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ) أَيْ: لِبَعْضِهَا، فَ فِي بِمَعْنَى: «اللام»، وَالمُرَادُ بـ «بَعْضِهَا: إِحْدَاهَا ; إِمَّا الصُّغْرَى وَإِمَّا الكُبْرَى، لَا مَا يَشْمَلُ جُزءاً مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ المُقَدِّمَةِ الوَاحِدَةِ. وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ أَنَّهُ لَا يَجُوزُ الحَذْفُ فِي كُلِّ المُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَهُوَ كَذَلِكَ حَيْثُ كَانَ المَقَامُ مَقَامَ اسْتِدْلَالٍ، كَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ.
الأنبابي: قوله : (وَهُوَ كَذَلِكَ حَيْثُ كَانَ المَقَامُ مَقَامَ اسْتِدْلَالٍ) قال العطّار هذا لا معنى له; لأنَّ المقام إمَّا خطابي، وإما استدلالي.
And deletion in some premises or the conclusion is for the sake of knowledge that is coming [later].
His statement: 'and deletion in some premises' – i.e., for some of them, so 'in' has the meaning of 'for' (lām). And what is meant by 'some of them' is one of them, either the minor or the major, not something that includes a part of a single premise. It is understood from the author's speech that deletion is not permissible in all premises, and that is so when the situation is one of inferential reasoning, as stated by some investigators.
Al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and that is so when the situation is one of inferential reasoning' – al-‘Attar said this has no meaning because the situation is either rhetorical or inferential.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of deleting a premise or the conclusion in a syllogism. The matn states that deletion of some premises or the conclusion is allowed for the sake of knowledge that is coming (i.e., it is understood or known from context). Al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies that 'in some premises' means deletion of one of them (minor or major), not a part of a single premise. He also deduces that deleting all premises is not allowed, and that this rule applies when the context is one of demonstrative reasoning (istidlāl). This is supported by some investigators. However, al-Anbabi (a super-commentator) seems to critique this last point, quoting al-‘Attar who says that the phrase 'when the situation is one of inferential reasoning' has no meaning because any situation is either rhetorical (khiṭābī) or inferential (istidlālī). This suggests a further refinement: perhaps the deletion rule applies only in rhetorical contexts, not in strict demonstration. The passage thus shows the ongoing scholarly discussion about the conditions under which omission is acceptable.

Key Terms
حذف
مقام استدلال

A) All premises
B) One premise (minor or major)
C) Any part of a premise
D) The conclusion only

Key Takeaway
Deletion of a single premise or the conclusion is permissible only when the missing part is understood, but deleting all premises is not allowed in inferential contexts.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines the second, third, and fourth figures of conditional syllogism based on the position of the middle term, and gives an example of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a conditional syllogism based on the position of the middle term (antecedent/consequent).

Core Idea
The figure of a conditional syllogism is determined solely by where the middle term sits in each premise.

In this passage, al-Bajuri completes the classification of conditional syllogisms by defining the second, third, and fourth figures based on the middle term's position as antecedent or consequent. The first figure is introduced through an example. Understanding these figures is essential for evaluating the validity of conditional arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the middle term in conditional premises
In conditional syllogisms, the middle term is the shared component between the two premises. It can be either the antecedent (if-part) or the consequent (then-part).
Identify the repeated term in the two premises.
2
Recognize the four possible positions
The middle term can be: (a) consequent in both premises (second figure), (b) antecedent in both (third figure), (c) antecedent in minor and consequent in major (fourth figure), or (d) consequent in minor and antecedent in major (first figure, implied by the example).
The first figure is the reverse of the fourth in order.
3
Map the example to the first figure
The example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' (minor: if rising then daytime) and 'not at all if daytime exists then night obtains' (major: if daytime then not night). The middle term 'daytime exists' is consequent in minor, antecedent in major. This matches the first figure pattern.
The conclusion would be: If sun rising, night does not obtain.
4
Apply the classification to any conditional syllogism
Given two conditional premises, locate the repeated term and note its position in each premise. Then assign the figure accordingly.
Practice with examples from kalam or usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباجوري - وَجَعْلَهُ تَالِياً فِيهِمَا يُسَمَّى : «شَكلاً ثَانِياً». - وَجَعْلَهُ مُقَدَّماً فِيهِمَا يُسَمَّى: «شَكلاً ثَالِثاً». - وَجَعْلَهُ مُقَدَّماً فِي الصُّغْرَى تَالِياً فِي الكُبْرَى يُسَمَّى : (شَكلاً رابعاً). فَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، وَلَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ النَّهَارُ مَوْجُوداً فَاللَّيْلُ حَاصِلٌ، وَعَلَى هَذَا القِيَاسُ.
Al-Bajuri: And making it (the middle term) a consequent in both is called 'second figure'. And making it an antecedent in both is called 'third figure'. And making it an antecedent in the minor premise and a consequent in the major premise is called 'fourth figure'. An example of the first figure is when you say: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists; and not at all if daytime exists then night obtains.' And upon this analogy.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of conditional syllogisms. In conditional syllogisms, the premises are conditional statements (e.g., 'If A then B') and the middle term is the shared part (either the antecedent or consequent in each premise). The author, responding to the earlier matn, specifies the second, third, and fourth figures: (1) second figure: the middle term is a consequent in both premises; (2) third figure: the middle term is an antecedent in both premises; (3) fourth figure: the middle term is an antecedent in the minor premise and a consequent in the major premise. The first figure is implied by the example: the middle term is a consequent in the minor and an antecedent in the major. Thus, the first figure is the reverse of the fourth figure in terms of order. The example given is a conditional syllogism in the first figure: minor premise 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' (middle term 'daytime exists' is consequent), major premise 'Not at all if daytime exists then night obtains' (middle term 'daytime exists' is antecedent), leading to the conclusion that if the sun is rising, night does not obtain. The author notes that all other examples follow this pattern.

شكل
تالٍ
مقدَّم

Key Takeaway
The figure of a conditional syllogism is defined by the position of the middle term (antecedent/consequent) in the two premises.

Therefore, if this is fermented grape juice, it is forbidden.' What figure is this? (Answer: first figure, middle term 'intoxicating' is consequent in first premise and antecedent in second premise.)

The Four Figures: Exclusive to Categorical Syllogisms
Author states the figures are only for categorical syllogism and allows omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the four figures (ashkāl) apply only to categorical syllogisms and identify when omission of a premise or conclusion is permissible.

Core Idea
Syllogistic figures are defined by the middle term; conditional syllogisms have their own distinct forms.

The four figures of the syllogism are a cornerstone of categorical logic, but they are not universal. The author precisely restricts them to categorical syllogisms (qiyas ḥamlī) and excludes conditional ones. Additionally, he notes that a premise or conclusion may be omitted when the omitted part is known or will be supplied. This lesson will help you internalize this domain restriction and the practical rule of omission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main restriction
Read the matn: 'wahadhihi al-ashkālu bi-l-ḥamlī mukhtaṣṣatun wa laysa bi-sh-sharṭī'. The author explicitly says these figures are specific to categorical syllogisms, not conditional ones.
The term 'ashkāl' here refers to the four figures (first through fourth) based on middle term position.
2
Understand the categorical vs. conditional distinction
In categorical syllogisms, premises are categorical propositions (subject-predicate). In conditional syllogisms, the premises are conditional propositions (if-then). The figures are defined by the middle term; conditional syllogisms have their own arrangements (e.g., modus ponens, modus tollens).
3
Analyze the omission permission
The matn says: 'wal-ḥadhfu fī ba‘ḍi l-muqaddimāti awi n-natījati li‘ilmin ātin'. This means it is permissible to omit some premises or the conclusion in expressing a syllogism, but only when the omitted part is known from context or will be provided later.
This is similar to an enthymeme in Western logic.
4
Consider the commentary's alternative reading
The commentator notes that 'bi-l-ḥamlī' could also mean 'by the categorical proposition' (rather than syllogism), as al-Mallawi suggested. This re-interpretation does not change the restriction but shows scholarly flexibility.
The commentary also says 'wa laysa bi-sh-sharṭī' is an explicit statement of what is already understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأشكال. وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِي مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي. وَالحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمٍ آتٍ. قَوْلُهُ : ( بالحَمْلِيّ) أَيْ: بِالقِيَاسِ الحَمْلِي. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ المُرَادَ - كَمَا أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ - : بِالقَضِيَّةِ الحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَتَذْكِيرُ المُصَنِّفِ لِتَأْوِيلِ القَضِيَّةِ بِالقَوْلِ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي) هَذَا تَصْرِيحُ بِمَا عُلِمَ.
Chapter on the Figures. And these figures are specific to the categorical (syllogism) and not to the conditional. And the omission of some of the premises or the conclusion is for a knowledge to come. His saying: 'by the categorical' means by the categorical syllogism. And it is possible that the intended meaning – as Shaykh al-Mallawi indicated – is the categorical proposition, and accordingly the author's masculinization is for interpreting the proposition as speech. His saying: 'and not by the conditional' is an explicit statement of what is known.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement from the matn clarifying the domain of the four figures. The author first states that these figures—the four figures of the syllogism—are specific to categorical syllogisms (qiyas hamli) and do not apply to conditional syllogisms (qiyas sharti). This is a foundational distinction: the figures are defined by the position of the middle term in categorical premises, but conditional syllogisms have their own figures based on the arrangement of the antecedent and consequent. The second part of the matn allows for the omission of some premises or the conclusion in the expression of a syllogism, but only for a known purpose—i.e., when the omitted part is obvious or will be supplied later. The commentary then explains the phrase 'bil-hamli' as meaning 'by the categorical syllogism' and also mentions an alternative interpretation by al-Mallawi that it refers to the categorical proposition. The commentary on 'wa laysa bi-sh-sharti' simply notes that this is an explicit clarification of what is already understood. The lesson emphasizes that the figures of the syllogism are inherently linked to categorical logic, and the rules of omission must be properly understood to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
أشكال
الحملِي
الشرطِي
الحذف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The four figures of the syllogism are exclusive to categorical syllogisms; conditional syllogisms have their own classification, and omission of premises or conclusion is allowed when the missing part is known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Five Forms of Omission in Syllogisms
Author enumerates and exemplifies the five forms of omission in syllogistic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five forms of omission (ḥadhf) in a syllogism and reconstruct the missing premises or conclusion in an enthymeme from Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
When a syllogism omits a premise, it does not lose its validity—it gains conciseness.

In everyday legal and theological argumentation, we rarely state every premise explicitly. Islamic logicians formalized this as 'ḥadhf' (omission). The passage you are about to study lists the five possible ways a syllogism can be truncated, and gives two examples from the punishment for fornication. Understanding these forms trains you to detect the logical skeleton behind any compressed argument.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the non-exclusive 'or'
Notice the author first clarifies that the word 'or' (أو) in the original statement does not indicate exclusive disjunction ('man'at jam''), meaning it is permissible to omit more than one element. This sets the stage for the full enumeration.
This clarification is crucial because it shows that the five forms include combinations, not just exclusive alternatives.
2
Enumerate the five forms
The five forms are: (1) omission of the minor premise alone, (2) omission of the major premise alone, (3) omission of the conclusion alone, (4) omission of the minor premise together with the conclusion, and (5) omission of the major premise together with the conclusion. Memorize these categories.
Note that 'each alone' actually covers three distinct forms (minor alone, major alone, conclusion alone), giving a total of five.
3
Analyze the first example (minor omitted)
The example 'Every fornicator is flogged, so Zayd is flogged' is a syllogism with the minor premise stated and the conclusion stated, but the major premise ('Zayd is a fornicator') is omitted. Identify the missing premise.
In context, the major premise is obvious from the claim being proven (that Zayd is flogged).
4
Analyze the second example (major omitted)
The example 'Because he is a fornicator, so he is flogged' states the major premise ('He is a fornicator') and the conclusion, but omits the minor premise ('Every fornicator is flogged'). Again, the missing premise is contextually known.
This shows how the same syllogism can appear in different truncated forms depending on which premise is already accepted.
5
Apply to any syllogism
Any complete categorical syllogism (two premises and a conclusion) can be expressed in any of these five truncated forms by omitting one or two elements. Practice by taking a simple Islamic legal syllogism and writing out all five versions.
Recognizing the form helps you supply the missing part correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَيْسَتْ مَانِعَةَ جَمْعِ أَيْضاً؛ لِجَوَازِ حَذْفِ البَعْضِ مَعَ النَّتِيجَةِ، فَتَلَخَّصَ مِنْ هَذَا : أَنَّ صُورَ الحَذْفِ خَمْسٌ : حَذْفُ كُلِّ وَحْدَهُ، وَحَذْفُ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ النَّتِيجَةِ، وَحَذْفُ الكُبْرَى مَعَهَا . فَحَذْفُ الصُّغْرَى وَحْدَهَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ - فِي مَقَامِ الِاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى دَعْوَى أَنَّ زَيْداً يُحَدُّ - : لأَنَّ كُلَّ زَانٍ يُحَدُّ، فَـ: زَيْدٌ يُحَدُّ. وَحَذْفُ الكُبْرَى وَحْدَهَا؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي هَذَا المَقَامِ : لأَنَّهُ زانٍ، فَـ: هُوَ يُحَدُّ.
And it is also not a conjunctive impossibility (man'at jam') because it is permissible to omit some with the conclusion. So from this it is summarized: that the forms of omission are five: omission of each alone, and omission of the minor with the conclusion, and omission of the major with it. So omission of the minor alone, as in your saying - in the context of seeking proof for the claim that Zayd is flogged -: 'Because every fornicator is flogged, so Zayd is flogged.' And omission of the major alone, as in your saying in this context: 'Because he is a fornicator, so he is flogged.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the five forms of omission in syllogistic reasoning. The author begins by clarifying that the word 'or' (أو) in the original statement about omitting some premises or the conclusion does not indicate exclusive disjunction (مانعة جمع), because it is permissible to omit both some premises and the conclusion together. From this analysis, the author concludes that there are exactly five forms of omission: (1) omission of each element individually (i.e., the minor premise alone, the major premise alone, or the conclusion alone), (2) omission of the minor premise together with the conclusion, and (3) omission of the major premise together with the conclusion. The author then provides two concrete examples from Islamic legal reasoning (hadd punishment for fornication). In the first example, the minor premise 'Every fornicator is flogged' is stated, and the conclusion 'Zayd is flogged' is implied; the major premise 'Zayd is a fornicator' is omitted. In the second example, the major premise 'He is a fornicator' is stated, and the conclusion 'He is flogged' is implied; the minor premise is omitted. These examples illustrate the practical use of enthymemes in legal argumentation, where one premise is left unstated because it is obvious or agreed upon.

Key Terms
حذف (ḥadhf): The deliberate omission of a premise or conclusion in a syllogism, leaving it implicit. [Note: No significant shift in meaning across the tradition; the concept is stable.]
صغرى (ṣughrā): The minor premise of a syllogism, containing the subject of the conclusion. [Note: Stable term across logical texts.]
كبرى (kubrā): The major premise of a syllogism, containing the predicate of the conclusion. [Note: Stable term.]
نتيجة (natīja): The conclusion of a syllogism, derived from the premises. [Note: Stable.]
مانعة جمع (māni'at jam'): An exclusive disjunction that prevents both disjuncts from being true simultaneously. [Note: In logic, this is a standard term for one type of disjunction; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The five forms of omission in syllogisms allow arguments to be concise while preserving logical validity, provided the missing premise is contextually known.

Pragmatic Deletion in Syllogisms
Commentary explains permissible deletions of premises or conclusion when known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise or conclusion has been deleted in an enthymematic syllogism and verify whether the deletion is permissible based on the listener's prior knowledge.

Core Idea
Brevity in argument is only permitted when the deleted part is already known to the audience.

In real debate, we rarely state every premise. Sometimes we omit one or even the conclusion for brevity. But this is only allowed if the listener already knows the omitted part. This lesson will show you how deletion works in Islamic argumentation and how to check its condition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the standard syllogism form
Recall the typical categorical syllogism: minor premise, major premise, conclusion. For example: 'Zaid is a fornicator; every fornicator is flogged; therefore Zaid is flogged.'
2
Identify what is deleted
In the first example: 'Because he is a fornicator, and every fornicator is flogged.' The conclusion 'therefore he is flogged' is missing. In the second: only 'every fornicator is flogged' — both minor and conclusion deleted. In the third: only 'because he is a fornicator' — major and conclusion deleted.
3
Apply the condition of known science
The phrase 'li'ilmin' means 'when the deleted part is known'. Check whether the listener already knows the missing proposition. If yes, deletion is allowed; if not, it is not permissible.
The comment clarifies that 'lam' here means 'when'.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider the argument: 'This liquid is nabidh; every intoxicant is haram; therefore this liquid is haram.' Try deleting the conclusion and see if the listener already knows that nabidh is intoxicating?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمٍ آتٍ. قَالَ الشَّارِحُ: وَحَذْفُ النَّتِيجَةِ وَحْدَهَا كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي الْمَقَامِ الْمَذْكُورِ: لِأَنَّهُ زَانٍ، وَكُلُّ زَانٍ يُحَدُّ. وَحَذْفُ الصُّغْرَى مَعَ النَّتِيجَةِ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْمَقَامِ: لِأَنَّ كُلَّ زَانٍ يُحَدُّ. وَحَذْفُ الْكُبْرَى مَعَهَا كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ فِي الْمَقَامِ الْمُتَقَدِّمِ: لِأَنَّهُ زَانٍ. وَيُحْفَظُ قَوْلُهُ: لِعِلْمٍ أَيْ عِنْدَ الْعِلْمِ بِالْمَحْذُوفِ، فَاللَّامُ بِمَعْنَى عِنْدَ. وَاحْتَرَزَ بِذَلِكَ عَمَّا إِذَا فَقَدَ الْعِلْمَ بِهِ فَلَا يَجُوزُ حَذْفُهُ.
And deletion in some of the premises or the conclusion is for a known science. The commentator said: And deletion of the conclusion alone is as in your saying in the mentioned context: 'Because he is a fornicator, and every fornicator is flogged.' And deletion of the minor premise with the conclusion is as in your saying in that context: 'Because every fornicator is flogged.' And deletion of the major premise with it is as in your saying in the preceding context: 'Because he is a fornicator.' And note his saying: 'for a known science' i.e., when the deleted part is known, so the lam is in the sense of 'when'. And he excluded by that the case when knowledge of it is absent, then it is not permissible to delete it.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line 'وَالْحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِعِلْمٍ آتٍ', which states that deletion of some premises or the conclusion is permissible when it is for a known science. The commentator explains that 'li'ilmin' means 'when there is knowledge of the deleted part'. He then provides three examples: deletion of the conclusion alone (as in 'because he is a fornicator, and every fornicator is flogged' — the conclusion 'therefore he is flogged' is omitted because it is known), deletion of the minor premise with the conclusion (only 'because every fornicator is flogged' is said, implying the known minor premise 'he is a fornicator'), and deletion of the major premise with the conclusion (only 'because he is a fornicator' is said, implying the known major premise 'every fornicator is flogged'). The key condition is that the deleted part must be known to the listener; if unknown, deletion is not permissible. This highlights the pragmatic nature of discourse in argumentation, where brevity is allowed when understanding is assured.

Reducing the Second Figure by Converting the Major Premise
Example of reducing second figure via major conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a valid second figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting its major premise, and verify that the conclusion remains unchanged.

Core Idea
Converting the major premise of second figure syllogisms aligns them with the first figure—but not all moods respond to this treatment.

This lesson focuses on the specific reduction method mentioned in the verses: the second figure is reduced by conversion of its major premise. We will walk through the example given in the commentary and see how the conversion allows the syllogism to fit the first figure, which is the standard for validity. Understanding this technique is essential for mastering syllogistic form and for evaluating whether a given argument can be simplified to the most straightforward figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure syllogism
Locate the middle term: in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. In the example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No stone is an animal'—the middle term 'animal' is predicate in both. The conclusion is 'No human is a stone'.
The example given is a valid second figure syllogism (Camestres-like).
2
Apply conversion to the major premise
The major premise is the universal negative: 'No stone is an animal'. Convert it by swapping subject and predicate, producing the equivalent 'No animal is a stone'. Conversion preserves truth and quality for universal negatives.
This is simple conversion ('aks).
3
Reconstruct the syllogism in the first figure
Now the premises are: 'Every human is an animal' (minor) and 'No animal is a stone' (major). The middle term 'animal' is now the subject of the major and predicate of the minor, fitting the first figure (subject-predicate order in major, predicate-subject in minor?). Actually, in the first figure the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor. Here: major: 'No animal is a stone' (subject: animal, predicate: stone); minor: 'Every human is an animal' (subject: human, predicate: animal). So the middle term 'animal' is subject in major and predicate in minor—that is the standard first figure pattern. The conclusion remains 'No human is a stone'.
The converted syllogism is first figure (Celarent).
4
Verify the conclusion is the same
Check that the reduced syllogism yields exactly the same conclusion as the original: 'No human is a stone'. This confirms that reduction by conversion of the major works for this mood.
The commentator notes this method works for only two moods of the second figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ فِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ زِيَادَةُ أَرْبَعَةِ أَبْيَاتٍ مُتَعَلِّقَةٍ بِرَدِّ مَا عَدَا الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ إِلَيْهِ وَنَصُّهَا: وَغَيْرُ أَوَّلٍ مِنَ الْأَشْكَالِ إِلَيْهِ مَرْدُودٌ بِلَا إِشْكَالِ فَالثَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى وَالثَّالِثُ أُرْدُدْهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَرَابِعُ بِعَكْسِ تَرْتِيبِ يُرَدْ أَوِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتُ هَكَذَا وَرَدْ وَأَوَّلُ مِنْهَا هُوَ الْمِعْيَارُ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ بَيْنِهَا الْمَدَارُ. وَالْمُتَبَادَرُ أَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْمُصَنِّفِ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ عَدَمِ الْاطِّرَادِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَضْرُبِ: إِذْ قَوْلُهُ: فَالثَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى لَا يَظْهَرُ إِلَّا فِي ضَرْبَيْنِ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ: الْأَوَّلُ وَالثَّالِثُ. فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الْأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ يَنْتُجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ. فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ كُبْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبَ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ.
And know that in some copies there is an addition of four verses related to reducing other than the first figure to it, and their text: 'And other than the first of the figures is reduced to it without difficulty; the second is reduced by conversion of its major, and the third reduce it by conversion of its minor, and the fourth is reduced by reversing the order, or the premises thus are reported. And the first of them is the standard because it is the axis among them.' The apparent understanding is that they are not from the author's words, because they lack consistency across all moods, since his saying: 'the second is reduced by conversion of its major' appears only in two of its moods: the first and the third. An example of the first mood: you say: 'Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal' yields 'No human is a stone.' So if you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its major by saying thus: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone' yields the same required conclusion, namely 'No human is a stone.'
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage discusses an additional four verses found in some manuscripts that describe how to reduce the second, third, and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism to the first figure. The commentator notes that these verses are likely not from the original author (al-Sullam) because they are not uniformly applicable. He points out that the rule for the second figure (reduction by conversion of the major) only works for two of its moods: the first mood (Cesare) and the third mood (Camestres?). He then gives an example of the first mood of the second figure: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone.' To reduce to the first figure, we convert the major premise 'No stone is an animal' to 'No animal is a stone' (simple conversion of a universal negative). Then we have a first figure syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, therefore no human is a stone.' This works because the converted major now has the middle term 'animal' in the predicate position, fitting the first figure pattern. The commentator implies that the reduction methods are not perfectly general across all moods, hence the verses are likely not original.

Reducing a second figure syllogism by converting its major premise can produce a first figure syllogism with the same conclusion, but this method is not universal across all moods.

Why the Reduction Verses Are Suspect: Limited Generality
Critique of the reduction verses' lack of generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which moods of the second figure can be reduced by converting the major premise, and explain why the reduction rule lacks generality.

Core Idea
The reduction rule for the second figure only works for two of its moods, revealing that the verses are likely a later addition.

The commentator alerts us that the four verses on reduction are probably not from the original author because the rule for the second figure—'reduction by conversion of the major'—does not apply consistently across all moods. In this lesson, we will examine why the rule fails for some moods, sharpening our understanding of the conditions under which reduction methods are valid.

Guided Path
1
List the four moods of the second figure
Recall the four standard moods: Cesare (EIO), Camestres (AEE), Festino (EIO), Baroco (AOO). They all have the middle term as predicate in both premises. Identify each by its premise forms.
The commentator mentions the first and third moods of the second figure—likely Cesare and Festino are the ones that work.
2
Test the reduction rule on each mood
For each mood, try to convert the major premise and see if the resulting syllogism fits a valid first figure mood. For example, for Cesare (No P is M, All S is M → No S is P): converting major gives No M is P, leading to first figure Celarent (No M is P, All S is M → No S is P). Works. For Camestres (All P is M, No S is M → No S is P): major is All P is M, converting gives All M is P? But conversion of universal affirmative (A) is not valid (it becomes particular). So reduction by simple conversion fails. Similarly for Baroco.
The method works only when the major premise is a universal negative (E), which can be simply converted.
3
Understand the commentator's reasoning
The commentator says the verses lack 'ittirād' (consistency) across all moods because 'the second is reduced by conversion of its major' appears only for two moods. He implies that authentic reduction rules should cover all moods of a figure. The fact that they don't suggests the verses are an interpolation.
This shows the critical rigor of the commentator in evaluating textual authenticity.
4
Consider alternative reduction methods
For moods where simple conversion fails, other methods like conversion with negation (obversion) or reductio ad impossibile can be used. The standard first figure reduction system includes these other techniques, but the verses only mention simple conversion.
This further supports the commentator's suspicion.

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ فِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ زِيَادَةُ أَرْبَعَةِ أَبْيَاتٍ مُتَعَلِّقَةٍ بِرَدِّ مَا عَدَا الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ إِلَيْهِ وَنَصُّهَا: وَغَيْرُ أَوَّلٍ مِنَ الْأَشْكَالِ إِلَيْهِ مَرْدُودٌ بِلَا إِشْكَالِ فَالثَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى وَالثَّالِثُ أُرْدُدْهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَرَابِعُ بِعَكْسِ تَرْتِيبِ يُرَدْ أَوِ الْمُقَدِّمَاتُ هَكَذَا وَرَدْ وَأَوَّلُ مِنْهَا هُوَ الْمِعْيَارُ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ بَيْنِهَا الْمَدَارُ. وَالْمُتَبَادَرُ أَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْمُصَنِّفِ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ عَدَمِ الْاطِّرَادِ فِي جَمِيعِ الْأَضْرُبِ: إِذْ قَوْلُهُ: فَالثَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى لَا يَظْهَرُ إِلَّا فِي ضَرْبَيْنِ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ: الْأَوَّلُ وَالثَّالِثُ. فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الْأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ يَنْتُجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ. فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ كُبْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبَ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ.
And know that in some copies there is an addition of four verses related to reducing other than the first figure to it, and their text: 'And other than the first of the figures is reduced to it without difficulty; the second is reduced by conversion of its major, and the third reduce it by conversion of its minor, and the fourth is reduced by reversing the order, or the premises thus are reported. And the first of them is the standard because it is the axis among them.' The apparent understanding is that they are not from the author's words, because they lack consistency across all moods, since his saying: 'the second is reduced by conversion of its major' appears only in two of its moods: the first and the third. An example of the first mood: you say: 'Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal' yields 'No human is a stone.' So if you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its major by saying thus: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone' yields the same required conclusion, namely 'No human is a stone.'
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage discusses an additional four verses found in some manuscripts that describe how to reduce the second, third, and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism to the first figure. The commentator notes that these verses are likely not from the original author (al-Sullam) because they are not uniformly applicable. He points out that the rule for the second figure (reduction by conversion of the major) only works for two of its moods: the first mood (Cesare) and the third mood (Camestres?). He then gives an example of the first mood of the second figure: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone.' To reduce to the first figure, we convert the major premise 'No stone is an animal' to 'No animal is a stone' (simple conversion of a universal negative). Then we have a first figure syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, therefore no human is a stone.' This works because the converted major now has the middle term 'animal' in the predicate position, fitting the first figure pattern. The commentator implies that the reduction methods are not perfectly general across all moods, hence the verses are likely not original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the reduction rule on the second figure mood known as Baroco (AOO: All P is M, Some S is not M → Some S is not P). Explain why converting the major premise (All P is M) does not yield a valid first figure syllogism.
?

The Syllogism

The Failure of Simple Reduction Rules
Critique of a poem on reducing figures to the first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a single rule for reducing all non-first figure syllogisms to the first figure is insufficient.

Core Idea
Not all moods reduce by the same simple conversion; each figure requires a tailored method.

This passage critiques a poem claiming that all non-first figures can be reduced to the first by a simple conversion. The author shows that the rules fail for several moods, highlighting the need for a nuanced approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem
Read the four-line poem presented in the passage and note its claims: second figure by conversion of major, third by conversion of minor, fourth by conversion of order.
2
Identify the author's initial reaction
The author states that the poem is likely not from the original author because its rules are not universally applicable.
3
Locate the specific criticism
The author says the rule for the second figure works only for two moods; find which moods these are.
4
Understand why generality fails
Reflect on why a rule that works for some moods fails for others: because the mood's formal properties (quantity and quality) affect the validity of the conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ فِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ زِيَادَةُ أَرْبَعَةِ أَبْيَاتٍ مُتَعَلِّقَةٍ بِرَدْ مَا عَدَا الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ إِلَيْهِ وَنَصُّهَا : وَغَيْرُ أَوَّلٍ مِنَ الأَشْكَالِ إِلَيْهِ مَرْدُودٌ بِلَا إِشْكَالِ فَالنَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى وَالثَّالِثُ أُرْدُدْهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَرَابِعُ بِعَكْسِ تَرْتِيبِ يُرَد أَوِ المُقَدِّمَاتُ هَكَذَا وَرَدْ وَأَوَّلُ مِنْهَا هُوَ المِعْيَارُ لأَنَّهُ مِنْ بَيْنِهَا المَدَارُ وَالمُتَبَادَرُ أَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ؛ لِمَا فِيهَا مِنْ عَدَمِ الاطِّرَادِ فِي جَمِيعِ الأَضْرُبِ : إِذْ قَوْلُهُ : فَالثَّانِ مَرْدُودُ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى لَا يَظْهَرُ إِلَّا فِي ضَرْبَيْنِ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ : الأَوَّلُ وَالثَّالِثُ : - فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ كُبْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ». وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّالِثِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانِ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ كُبْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَجَرٍ. وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ ظَاهِراً فِي كُلٍّ مِنَ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي وَالرَّابعِ: - لِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الثَّانِي مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ سَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ صُغْرَاهُ سَالِبَةٌ وَكُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتَاجِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ. وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلَهَا كُبْرَى ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ أَنْتَجَ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعَلْتَهَا كُبْرَى، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، فَتَعْكُسُ النَّتِيجَةَ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانِ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ». - وَلِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الرَّابِعَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ سَالِبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى، لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ؛ لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِدَلِيلِ الافْتِرَاضِ، وَقَدْ بَيَّنَهُ الإِمَامُ السَّنُوسِيُّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ بِمَا يَطُولُ ذِكْرُهُ . وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالثَّالِثُ أُرْدُدْهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى لَا يَظْهَرُ إِلَّا فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَضْرُبٍ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ : الأَوَّلُ، وَالثَّانِي، وَالثَّالِثُ، وَالخَامِسُ . - فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلٌّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: «بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ». - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ
And know that in some copies there is an addition of four lines related to reducing other than the first figure to it, and their text is: 'And other than the first of the figures to it is reduced without difficulty. So the second is reduced by conversion of the major, and the third reduce it by conversion of the minor, and the fourth by conversion of the order is reduced, or the premises thus are reduced. And the first of them is the standard because among them it is the pivot.' And the apparent view is that they are not from the words of the author; because of what is in them of lack of generality in all the moods: since his saying: 'So the second is reduced by conversion of the major' appears only in two of its moods: the first and the third. So an example of the first mood is that you say: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing of stone is an animal' yields 'Nothing of human is stone.' So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its major by saying thus: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing of animal is stone' yields the same desired conclusion, which is 'Nothing of human is stone.' And an example of the third mood is that you say: 'Some animal is human, and nothing of stone is human' yields 'Some animal is not stone.' So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its major by saying thus: 'Some animal is human, and nothing of human is stone' yields the same desired conclusion, which is 'Some animal is not stone.' And it was not apparent in each of the second and fourth moods because the second mood is composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the major, it would be among its sterile moods; because its minor is negative and its major is particular, and it has preceded that for productivity of the first figure, positivity of the minor and universality of the major are required. So it cannot be reduced by that. And it can only be reduced by conversion of the minor and making it the major, then conversion of the conclusion. So if you say, for example: 'Nothing of stone is an animal, and every human is an animal' yields 'Nothing of stone is human.' So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert the minor and make it the major, then convert the conclusion by saying thus: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing of animal is stone' yields 'Nothing of human is stone.' Then you convert the conclusion to our saying: 'Nothing of stone is human,' which is the desired conclusion. And because the fourth mood is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the major, it would be among its sterile moods; due to what preceded, so it cannot be reduced by that. And it can only be reduced by the proof of hypothesis, and Imam al-Sanusi explained it in his commentary on his Mukhtasar with a lengthy account. And his saying: 'And the third reduce it by conversion of the minor' appears only in four of its moods: the first, second, third, and fifth. So an example of the first mood: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' yields 'Some animal is rational.' So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying thus: 'Some animal is human, and every human is rational' yields the same desired conclusion, which is 'Some animal is rational.' And an example of the second mood: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing of human is a horse' yields 'Some animal is not a horse.' So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying thus: 'Some animal is human, and every human is not a horse?' [end of available text]
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of a four-line poem that purports to give simple rules for reducing all non-first figures to the first figure. The commentator, al-Bajuri, first presents the poem and then states the apparent opinion that the poem is not from the original author, because its rules do not apply universally across all moods of each figure. He then systematically demonstrates the deficiencies. For the second figure, the poem claims that conversion of the major premise suffices. Al-Bajuri shows this works only for the first and third moods of the second figure. For example, in the first mood (universal negative conclusion), converting the major ('No animal is stone' to 'No stone is animal') and then using it as the minor in the first figure yields the correct conclusion. In the third mood (particular negative conclusion), the same method works. However, for the second and fourth moods, this simple conversion fails. The second mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major; converting the major would make the major particular (since conversion of an A proposition gives I), which violates the first-figure requirement that the major be universal. Instead, the correct reduction involves converting the minor (making it the new major) and then converting the conclusion. Al-Bajuri provides an example: from 'No stone is an animal' and 'Every human is an animal', the conclusion 'No stone is human' can be reached by first converting the minor to 'Some animal is stone' (actually careful: 'No stone is animal' converts to 'No animal is stone'? Wait, conversion of E is E: 'No stone is animal' becomes 'No animal is stone'. Then we have 'Every human is animal' and 'No animal is stone', yielding 'No human is stone', then converting the conclusion gives 'No stone is human'. So the method works. For the fourth mood (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), even this method fails; it requires the more advanced 'proof by hypothesis' (dalil al-iftirad), which al-Bajuri notes was explained by al-Sanusi at length. Turning to the third figure, the poem says to use conversion of the minor. Al-Bajuri shows this works for four moods: first, second, third, and fifth. He gives examples for the first mood (AAA: Every human is animal, every human is rational yields some animal is rational) by converting the minor to 'Some animal is human', and for the second mood (EAO: Every human is animal, no human is horse yields some animal is not horse) again by converting the minor. The overall lesson is that the reduction rules are not as simple as the poem suggests; different moods require different techniques, and some require more complex proofs.

Key Takeaway
Simple reduction rules often fail; one must understand the specific requirements of each mood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider this second-figure syllogism from a kalam proof: 'No matter is eternal, and every body is matter' (yielding 'No body is eternal'). How would you reduce this to the first figure? Apply the correct method (conversion of major or minor) and check if it works.

Second Figure Reduction: Conversion of the Major and Its Limits
Reduction of the second figure in detail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which moods of the second figure can be reduced by converting the major premise and which cannot, and apply the alternative method for the second mood.

Core Idea
Second figure reduction is not uniform: the second and fourth moods require different methods than the first and third.

The poem claims the second figure is reduced by converting the major premise. This works for the first and third moods but fails for the second and fourth. The author explains why and provides the correct reduction for the second mood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two moods where conversion of major works
The first mood (universal negative conclusion) and third mood (particular negative conclusion). Note their premise patterns.
2
Examine the first mood example
From 'Every human is animal, no stone is animal' to 'No human is stone'. Converting the major gives 'No animal is stone', then rearranging yields a valid first-figure syllogism.
3
Examine the third mood example
From 'Some animal is human, no stone is human' to 'Some animal is not stone'. Converting the major gives 'No human is stone', then using it as the major with the original minor yields the conclusion.
4
Understand why the second mood fails
The second mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major. Converting the major (A to I) makes the major particular, violating the first-figure condition that the major must be universal.
5
Apply the alternative method for the second mood
Example: 'No stone is animal, every human is animal' yields 'No stone is human'. Reduce by converting the minor (E to E) to 'No animal is stone', then make it the major, and convert the conclusion.

Source Text
